REPUBLIC OF TURXEY
SECOND HEALTH PROJECTs
ESSENTIAL HEALTH SERVICES AND MANAGEMENT DEVELOPMENT
IN EASTERN AND SOUTHEASTERN ANATOEIA
L.oun and Project 8ummary
BORROWERs         Republic of Turkey
BENEFrCrARIESa   Min$stry of Health (MOH) departments, provincial health
directorates, health care units in the project provinces,
pilot hospitals and selected universities.
LOAN AMOUNTs      US$150.0 million equivalent.
TERWSt            Seventeen years, including a five-year grace period, at the
IBRD standard variable interest rate.
PROJECT
OBJkCTIVEs        The primary goal of the Second Health Project is to red4ce
the burden of disease due to avoidable illness and
disability in Turkey.  To achieve this goal, the specific
objectives of the Project are to: (a) improve equity of
access tq essential health services in 23 eastern, low-
income, priority provinces; and (b) improve the quality of
health care management in selected institutions.
PRQ=
DESCRIPTION:      The proposed Prcject will provide fellowships, training,
technical assistance, studies, health programs, computer
hardware and software, medical and office furniture,
equipment, vehicles, teaching materials, civil works and
non-salary incremental recurrent costs. The Project, which
covers 16 percent of Turkey's total population (11.8 million
people in 1993) and 29 percent of its surface area, will be
implemented over seven and a half years through the
following two components (US$200.0 million total cost):
The Primary Health Care Cogponent (US$155.6 million total
cost) will improve equity in access to essential health
services by: (a) upgrading the traiiing of primary health
care personnel; (b) expanding basic health care
interventions known to be cost-effective; and
(c) strengthening the network of health care facilities to
support the above activities. This component forms the
centerpiece of the Second Health Project. It sets
priorities based on the leading causes of mortality and
morbidity, and it will keep these at the forefront of
debates on reforms in the health care system.
The Health Policy and Wanaaement Component (US$44.4 million
total cost) will improve the quality of health care
management in selected institutions bys (a) upgrading the
training of staff in policymaking, planning, management,



- ii -
monitoring and evaluation; (b) decentralizing and
restructuring management responsibility in six pilot
hospitalsl (c) introducing basic management support systems
in Provincial Health Directorates and selected hospitals in
the project provinces, as well as evaluating a more
comprehensive management information system in one urban
pilot hospital in western Turkey; and (d) conlucting pre-
investment studies to support broader reforms in the health
sector. Activities supported under (b) and (c) are
explicitly designed as trial runs for future countrywide
implementation.
BENEFITS:         The Project's major benefit will be to reduce the number of
avoidable deaths and disability in the project provinces,
especially among women and childr-n. Targeted investments
in training and facilities will lead to better staffed and
equipped essential health services in these provinces. Such
interventions are well known to be cost-effective and a
precondition for improving maternal and child survival. A
second benefit of the Project will be to obtain better value
for money spent on health services. Staff in the project
provinces will be trained in health services management and
provided with management support systems. This will allow
them to improve the quality of scrvices provided and avoid
wasting scarce resources.
RI8R8S            A major risk faced by the Project is that it may become
difficult to implement and supervise activities in a number
of the project provinces which currently have accessibility
problems. Contingency plans are included to allow: (a) out-
of-province training where necessary; (b) pro-selection of
only urban facilities for new civil works to ensure t1e
possibility of an adequate supervision, staffing and supply
of equipment; (c) province-by-province costing to allow
flexibility under a phased implementation plan; and (d) use
of a professional engineering firm to oversee the
construction programs. A second risk is the limited
institutional capacity of MOH departments, especially in the
area of training. This risk will be mitigated by:
(a) appropriate deployment of staff trained through
fellowships financed under the First Health Project;
(b) technical assistance, including management contracts for
logistic support in coordinating training activities;
(c) decentralization of training efforts to provincial
teams; (d) modular distance-based education; (e) improved
coordination of the different General Directorates in an
integrated approach to training activities; and
(f) collaboration with universities as co-participants in
the implementation of training programs.

REPUBLIC OE TURKEY
SECOND HEALTH PROJECT:
ESSENTIAL HEALTH SERVICES AND  EANA              MENT
IN EASTERN AND SOUTHEASTERN ANATOLIA
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. THE HEALTH OF SHE TURKISH POPULATION
1.1. Investments in health, education and poverty alleviation in Turkey have
brought with them improvements in economic growth and human welfare.
Conversely, economic growth, manifested in a four-fold increase in GNP per
capita (US$2,130 in 1993) during the past thirty years, has contributed
significantly to improvements in the health of the population. Although
regional and urban/rural disparities still exist among the country's 59.9
million people, a better supply of food, clean water, safe sanitation, decent
housing and basic education is now available to many of those who were
previously deprived of these fundamental necessities of life. In the health
sector, the number of doctors, nurses, clinics and hospitals has increased
several fold during this time period. The impact of these factors on health
has been impressive: ave,age life expectancy at birth has increased by over 40
percent to reach 67 years in 1991; infant mortality has dropped by 72 percent
to reach 58 per thousand; and the number of deaths due to infectious diseases
has decreased significantly. As a result, global health indicators In Turkey
have Improved to a point where many are now comparable to those of other
countries with a similar per capita income.
1.2. Despite these achievements, the burden of disease in Turkey remains
high. For example, although the country ranks 69th in the world in terms of
income per capita, it ranks 91st in terms of under-five mortality rates. This
statistic reflects regional disparities in the health and status of the
country's women, in education of parents, in prevalence of maleutrition, in
availability of clean water and in the overall qual'ty of health services as
well as differences in standards of living. The number of children who die
before the age of five from preventable conditions such as improper
deliveries, poor feeding practices, uncomplicated diarrhea, pneumonia and
accidents indicates that the country has not yet maximized the potential
advantages of its economic development, nor benefitted fully from investments
made in the health sector during recent years.
1.3. Ninety percent of the critical development of human beings occurs In the
first few years of life. Each child has only one chance to capture the full
developmental benefit of the intrxate processes of that growth. Countries
that fail today to maintain the basic needs of women and children as a first
priority within their development strategies inflict irreversible damage on
their human capital of tomorrow. Improving the effectiveness, efficiency and
equity of the 4.6 percent of GNP (US$98 per capita per year) currently
consumed by the health sector in Turkey has, therefore, become a very high
priority. The Second Health Project will support the Government in achieving
this objective in the country's most deprived 23 eastern provinces where the
burden of poverty, disease and disability is the greatest.



- 2 -
A.  General/Sector Background
Recent Milestones in Improving Health
1.4. Overall health in Turkey has improved more in the past three to four
decades than it did during all the rest of the country's history. In 1960,
average life expectancy at birth was 47: in 1991 it was 67. Child mortality
rates (the number of children who die between the ages of 1 and 5) dropped
during t.he same period from 21.7 to 9.4 percent; and infant mortality rates
(the number of deaths during the first year of life) dropped from 20.8 to 5.8
percent. The number of women dying from complications related to pregnancy is
thought to have dropped to 132 per 100,000 from much higher levels. An
increase in the legal age of marriage, use of contraceptives, better education
for schoolgirls and women, changes in cultural norms and increases in life
expectancy have also contributed to a steady fall in total fertility, with the
crude birth rate dropping from 3.6 to 2.8 percent between 1970 and 1991 and
the national average total fertility rate dropping from 4.9 to 3.4 percent.
These trends more than offset the concomitant decrease in the crude death rate
from 1.2 to 0.7 percent over the same time period.L/
1.5. How and where people live and raise their children have a profound
effect on health. The marked growth and urbanization of the Turkish
population over the past two decades has had a major impact on bczh health
atatus and disease patterns throughout the country. In 1970, only 38 percent
of the population lived in urban areas. By 1991, this figure had risen to 63
percent. Enrollment in primary schools has increased from less than 70
percent of the age group in 1950 to over 100 percent currently, with female
enrollments groving most rapidly in recent years.2/ Better education,
especially for women and girls, has increased the populationws understanding
of the leading threats to good health, the ability to care for their families
and the probability that they will seek appropriate access to health services.
These combined factors have led to marked improvements in standards of living
and reduction in the morbidity and mortality from many diseases.
Foundations for a National Health Service
1.6. Although the technical solutions to most preventable hetlth conditions
have been known for many years, establishing an effective health service
depends not only on appropriat. and affordable technology, but also on a
sustained political commitment , positive change, the availability of quality
health facilities and adequate financing. There has been significant prog--ess
in providing these basic building blocks in Turkey during the past few decades.
/    These improvemnts in health in Turkey reflect similar worldwide trends during the 20th century,
such that within a decade it would be possible to control most major childhood diseases, cut
malnutrition in haltf, provide alt children with a basic education, and bring clean water, sanitary
housing and family planning to atl communities. According to UNICEF. the global cost would be only
US$25 billion a year, or half of what Europeans spend each year on cigarettes, less than what
Americans spend annually on beer.
This overstates coverage in that it includes many over-age students. It also hides the fact that a
large nrwber of children, especially girls in rural areas, stilt do not attend school.



- 3 -
1.7. Political Commitment. Since establishment of the State Planning
Organization (SPO) In 1960, six successive Five-Year Plans (FYP) have aimed to
Improve equity in access to health services for the TurLish population.]/ The
1961 Basic Health Law (Law No. 224) reorganized the health sector into a
hierarchical network of public institutions that were staffed by civil
servants along the lines of the British Natioaal Health Service (NBS). The
plan was financed through premiums paid by vorkers and subsidies from the
state budget for the inactive or poor part of the population. The right of
every citizen to live in a healthy and balanced environment was later written
into the Constitution of the Turkish Republic in 1982. The 1987 Basic Health
Law (Law No. 3359) attempted to address areas where the 1961 Law had failed to
achieve its objectives, and strengthened the Turkish National Health Service
run by the Ministry of Health (MOH). The Government's current health policy
is described in Section C, below.
1.8. Underlying Tnfrastructure. Today, the NOR is the largest provider of
health care, and practically the only provider of health promotion and disease
prevention services, essential health services for low income populations, and
highly specialized tertiary care. Of the country's 970 hospitals (2.4 beds
per thousand population), 634 are run by the MOH, 105 by the Social Security
Institution (SSK)j/ for non-public-sector wage earners, 25 by universities,
and the remaining 206 by other semi-autonomous organizations such as the
Ministry of Defense, .ather ministries, state economic enterprises,
municipalities and the private sector. At the central levAl. the MOH is
responsible for health policy and planning. At lower levels of the system,
the provincial Health Directorates, which report to the provincial Governor,
are responsible.
1.9. Guided by the objectives and action plans that were set forth by
successive FYPs, the Turkish health sector has undergone a significant
expansion of its manpower and infrastructure for essential health services
during the past 30 years.jA/ The average population per physician dropped from
1,630 in 1980 to 1,180 in 1990. Similar improvements were observed in several
other health professions, leaving the health sector in 1990 with 8.5 doctors,
6.0 nurses, 3.5 midwives, 1.6 dentists and 2.3 pharmacists per 10,000
population, which is within the low range of norms observed in other OECD
countries (7 to 35 per 10,000 population for doctors, 6 to 173 for nurses, one
to 11 for dentists and 1.4 to 11 for pharmacists). By 1991, there were 11,490
health posts for midwives serving populatious of 2,500 to 3,000 in rural
areas, compared with 2,230 In 1970. The number of health centers serving
populations of 5 to 10 thousand rose to 3,091 from 851 over the same period.
The number of out-patient consultations has increased 8.5 times since 1960,
while the use of in-patient services has increased only 3.5 times.
I/    A planned approach to developments in the health sector has prevailed since the first FYP which was
prepared in 1933, long befo-e the six most recent FYPs which were prepared after the creation of the
SPO.
V/   Sosyal Sigortalar Kurunu.
5/   See Statistical Tables and Figures following Annex S for a comparison of health care facilities in
Turkey and other OECD countries.



1.10. Finaneing. Finally, the last 30 years have also witnessed an
improvement In the financing of health services. When the Basic Health Care
Law was introduced in 1961, public provisions for protection against the
financial hardships created by illness were virtually unknown to most of the
population. Today, approximately 39 million people, or 68 percent of the
population, are eligible for benefits under one of the three major publicly
subsidized and employment-based social insurance schemes, SSK, Emekli Sandlgi
and Bag-Kur. The remaining 32 percent of the population pay for their care
out-of-pocket or, if poor, receive free care in MOH facilities.
B. KeylIssues
Continued Burden of Disease
1.11. Despite the above achievements, progress has been uneven and the
challenge of removing the remaining obstacles to good health in Turkey is
great. Skewed economic and social development has left many regions of the
country behind. This results in the unnecessary death and disability of
thousands of people, especially women and children, each year.
1.12. Skeved Socioegonpmic Development. The links between well-being and
national development are well known. Historically, development has always been
greater in the western part of the country where access to basic
infrastructure, such as roads, drinking water, sanitatioi. electricity and
telecommunications is much better than in the central and eastern provinces.
1.13. The usual breakdown of the country into five geographical regions does
not function well as a basis for classifying the provinces according to socio-
economic development (see Map 1). To over-ome this problem, the SPO has
grouped the country s provinces into the following four different regions
based on demographic, income, employment, education, morbidity and mortality
indicators (see Map 2):h/ (a) first order priority provinces (PPl) from
Eastern and Southeastern Anatolia, which constitute 11 percent of the
country's population and contribute less than three percent to GDP; (b) second
order priority provinces (PP2), from the Eastern, Central and Northern
Anatolian Region just west of the PP1 provinces, which constitute 15 percent
of the population and less than nine percent of GDP; (c) provinces with medium
levels of development; and (d) developed provinces. In the PPI provinces, 60
percent of children are cared for by women who cannot read, while the primary
school attendance rate for girls is as low as 52 percent.
1.14. Preventable Deaths. Although infant mortality and under-five child
mortality in Turkey have decreased dramatically during recent years to settle
at rates of 5.8 and 7.8 percent, respectively, in 1990, these levels are still
high by international standards and there are considerable regional
differences. The high infant mortality rates reflect higher-than-average
fertility, poor ante-natal and post-natal care, improper deliveries, poor
Wt   Many of the PPI and PP2 provinces have forbidding climate and geographic conditions. More than
sixty percent of their total area is above an altitude of 2,300 meters, reaching 5,137 meters in the
Agri Mountains.



feeding practices, low educational status and environmental factors. Vhile 47
infants per thousand die in the western part of the country before reaching
the age of one, they die at rates four times higher in some areas of the most
deprived eastern PP1 and PP2 provinces. With total Lertility rates of seven
In urban areas and 10 in rural areas, there are 1.22 million children In the
under-five age group in the PP1 provinces. The average under-five mortality
rate of 11.7 percent for this age grout will result in over 28.5 thousand of
those children dylng annually before reaching the age of five.j/ In the PP2
provinces, the under-five mortality rate of 9.2 will result in a death toll In
the under-five age group of 25 thousand per year.
1.15. Likewise, despite considerable progress ln recent years, improvements
observed in womews health and maternal mortality have not attained the
targeted levels. About half of all maternal deaths occur during delivery,
one-fourth during pregnancy ard one-fourth after pregnancy. Preventable
factors that increase the risk of maternal mortality are still prevalent,
Including: poor education, high fertility rates, poor birth spacing, early
teenage and later age pregnancies, multiple deliveries, malnutrition, and
inadequate access to appropriate health care for women (ante-natal care, safe
deliveries, close post-partum follov-up and family planning).
Xneguity in Access to Cost-Effective Services
1.16. Without access to effective and affordable health services, praiseworthy
policies intended to promote good health and fulfill constitutional rights to
health care have little meaning to the average citizen. In this respect, the
Turkish health system is still plagued by a number of very serious problems.
1.17. Distorted Prioritles in Training. In the past, the training of doctors
and other health personnel failed to provide them with the right skills or
incentives to deliver effective care in priority areas such as primary health
care in the deprived eastern provinces of the country. The curriculum of most
medical training programs remains weak in maternal and child care, although
the most cost-effective interventions to reduce the burden of disease in
Turkey rely on skilled primary health care personnel and active *,ommuity
participation. Young doctors and other health care workers find it more
prestigious and financially rewarding to train for work in hospitals and
specialized clinics than for work in the primary care setting. As a result,
the population per physician ranges from 1,939 (Elazig) to 8,650 (Van) and
population per mi.;: fe ranges from 597 (Artvin) to 8,238 (Van). The distance
that patients need to travel to the neare-t health center or hospital often
reaches twice or more the regional average (15 km for health center and 41 km
for hospituls) for the PP1 and PP2 provinces.
1.18. Poor Value for goney. Despite the existence of cost-effective and
affordable ways to prevent and treat many of the country's leading health
problems, too often such interventions are not an Integral part of essential
health services. Spending by the MOH, the principal provider of primary
v     This figure was derived by mutliplying the total number of children under-five (1.2 million) by the

health care, was less than 20 percent of total health care expendLture of 20.8
trilliou. TL In 1991 (11.7 trillion for public provision and 9.1 trillion for
private provision). Less than 18 percent of total public expenditure (NOR,
88K and other inistrLes) Is spent on essential health services, and less than
four percent is spent on health promotion and disease prevention activities.
Self-help groups, EGOs and community health workers are not well integrated
into the formal network of health services. The combined effect of lack of
appropriate training, high staff turnover rates, shortages of essential
equipment, supplies and drugs and poor transportation prevents essential
health servlces in Turkey from functioning effectively in this regard. This
leads to widespread reliance on specialized and institutional care for cases
that could easily be treated by well-trained primary care or community workers
in properly equipped facilities.
1.19. Paradox of Shortafes and Underutilization. In the PP1 and PP2
provinces, there is often a lack of even the most basic health care
infr.astructure and equipment. Despite efforts to correct such inequities,
on?y 45 percent of the villages in this part of the country have health posts
for midwives and health centers.!/ More than one-third of all hospital beds
are found in three major urban centers (Ankara, Istanbul and Izmir) which
comprLse less than a quarter of the country's population. At the same time,
nearly half of all doctors (33 percent of all doctors working for the MOH),
practLce In the same three major urban centers, while only 16 percent of NOR
doctors practice in the priority provinces, which comprise 25 percent of the
population. Although unreported visits may lead to undercounting, out-patient
attendance is only two to three times a year on average (less than one for
women and children) with a range of two to 13 for OECD countries. The average
length-of-stay in Turkish hospitals is 6.9 days compared with 9.9 days for
acute care and 32.2 days for chronic care in Western Europe. Occupancy rates
are 55 percent in MOH hospitals, 72 percent for the SSK, and 37 to 75 percent
for university hospitals. This is low by European standards. Shortages in
personnel, equipment, supplies, and vehicles, as well as poor access to
appropriate health care facilities, are major contributing factors.
InefficLent Use of Scarce Resources
1.20. A lack of proper training in health policy and management, the rigid
structure of the health service, and the absence of modern management support
systems contribute significantly to the underlying structural problems in the
health sector, leading to a great waste of scarce resources.
1.21. Inanuropriate Training in Manafement. Fev managers in the health sector
have adequate training In health policy and planning, health services
management, data processing, human resources, time management, applied
epidemiology and health economics, or quality assurance. The national health
service was built and operates according to a centralized planALng model, with
many key decisions still being taken by the NOR in Ankara, despite previous
attempts by the Government to decentralize the health service. Formal lines
Av   In sawe of the provinces chosen for the Project, thfs figure is as low as 20 percent (see Chapter II
for a detailed description of the project provinces).



- 7 -
of authority at the central level are unclear and coordination between the
thirty-odd providers of health services in the public sector and the private
sector Ls poor. Even within a single subsector, vertically designed programs
for essential health services are covered by several Directorates ln the NOR.
In the prlmary care settlng, where there is often no institutional framework
to provide support, lack of management skills is a particular handlcap.
1.22. fureauciacv Vithout Management. In hospitals, day-to-day management
responsibLlities are delegated to the Chief Doctor, usually a prominent
clinician who continues to practice his specialty and who has little If any
training, skills, or inclination for management, planning and evaluation.
Likewise, the Head Nurse is required to have 10 years of service, but no
corresponding management skills or training. The administratlve
responsibilities of the Hospital Administrator and Treasurer, who work as
deputies to the Chief Doctor, are limited to running the revolving fund,
accounting office, laundry, kitchen, power supply, transportation and other
support services. Hospital administrators are trained to know the Hospital
Administration Law, but their true skills in management are limited.
1.23. Manual Record Keenin2 and Obsolete Technology. In addition to the above
shortcomings in the structure and management of the health sector, many
services are poorly run, relying largely on manual record keeping and obsolete
technology. Few hospitals or health centers have access to management-based
information systems to track health outcomes, performance and expenditure
trends, or to assist in record keeping (admission/discharge data), accounting,
personnel management, materials management, equipment management and
monitoiing of utilization patterns. This prevents hospital staff from making
informed decisions and allocating resources according to real priorities
rather than by normative standards. It also forces highly specialized
clinical workers to waste a great deal of their time performing administrative
duties rather than providing medical care.
C.  Government Health Sector Strategy
1.24. The Government of Turkey is deeply committed to improving the health of
the population and the performance of the health sector in an attempt to
secure the well-being of its population and to foster continued growth and
development of its economy. The current Sixth PYP, which began in 1990, gives
special priority to the protection and education of women and children as the
cornerstone of the socioeconomic development of Turkish society.2/ These
objectives were articulated in more concrete terms in a policy document which
was published in 1993.1Q/ The document emphasizes the need to create an
enabling environment for improving health through poverty reduction, better
education and improved nutrition. It also emphasizes the need to reduce
2/   The sixth FYP also included goals outside the formal health sector, such as inprovements in incame
distribution, reductions in unemployment and a decrease in regional development disparities, all of
ihich have a significant inpact on health.
jfl   Republic of Turkey, Ministry of Health (NOR), uPreparing for the Third Nillemium: National Health
Policy in Turkey," Ankara: NON, 1993.



regional disparities, particularly in health services, education and
agriculture, all of which are critical to improving the health status.
1.25. In the past, similar broad reforms of the health sector consistently
failed because they lacked the necessary legal framework, institutlonal
structure and financial support. Determined not to repeat this mistake, the
Government's strategy for realizing the underlying objectives of its reform
program lncludes: (a) strengthening the basic infrastructure and health care
programs available to vulnerable populations in the low-income provinces of
Eastern and Southeastern Anatolia through support from the Second Health
Project; (b) continuing feasibility studies needed to design a broader reform
of the health sector through support from both the First and Second Health
Projects; and (c) conducting a thorough review of the social insurance system
(pension and health insurance) with support from the Privatization
Implementatior. Assistance Project (3728-TU).
1.26. Once the foundation for a more systemic reform of the health sector bas
been completed, the Government intends to introduce its long-promised
Integrated Health Care Reform (IHCR) Program. This program, which ls still
being elaborated, will focus mainly on creating a general health insurance
scheme to cover the non-insured population, regionalization of health
services, decentralization of management responsibilities in hospitals, and
strengthening of the role of family medicine in providing essential health
services. However, without first introducing the needed infrastructure in
terms of primary health care training, personnel and facilities, the
introduction of further reforms in the health sector would be little more than
"paper guarantees." The proposed Second Health Project will continue the
process started under the First Health Project in providing these bullding
blocks, thereby paving the way for an eventual major structural reform in the
health sector which could also be supported by a World Bank loan.
D. Past World Bank Involvement in the Health Sector
1.27. The World Bank first became involved in supporting the Government's
heelth sector strategy during the mid-1980s. This led to the preparation of
the First Health Project (Loan 3057-TU for US$75 million) which became
effective in October 1990 (see Annex 5 for a more detailed description). 4
The objectives of the First Health Project, which is still under
implementation, were to: (a) improve health status for underprivileged
populations through better access to basic health services in eight of the
country's provinces (10 percent of the population in 1990);1Z/ (b) enhance
efficiency In service delivery and improve health care financing; and
(c) strengthen the management capacity of the MOH. The First Health Project
had a slow start-up. This was due primarily to four factors: (i) lack of
familiarity with Bank procedures; (ii) frequent changes in senior management
of the NOH; (iii) failure to define clearly the role of the Project
1y   See also, First Health Project, Staff Appraisal Report 7433TU, June 29, 1990 and Semi-Annust

Coordination Unit (PCU); and (iv) weak institutional capacity to develop and
implement large in-service training programs. Within the past 12 months.
considerable progress has been made In addressing all of these problems. The
PCU is now fully operational and all subcomponents of the Project are being
implemented. As of July 31, 1994, about US$16.14 million of the Bank loan
(21.5 percent) has been disbursed and another US$13.3 million has been
committed.
E. Rationale for Continued World Bank Involvement
1.28. Continued World Bank involvement in the health se .tor in Turkey is
justified for several reasons. First, it is consistent with the Bank's
overall strategy for health and development which is expressed in the 1993
World Development Report (WDR), Investing in Health.  Like other World Bank
reports, the 1993 WDR highlights the important interplay between poverty
relief, basic education, economic development and health status. Consistent
wLth this strategy, the Bank has supported a number of projects in Turkey that
aim at providing clean water and an adequate supply of food, both of which are
iziispensable prerequisites for good health.13/ Several Bank supported
training and education projectslj/ increase the chance that people will lead
healthier lifestyles and know when to seek appropriate health care. Measures
to strengthen the social safety net W and the essential role women play in
developmentli/ will have an equally important impact on improvszg health.
Since poor health has a negative effect on labor productivity, contributes to
unemployment, and prevents children from attending school, breaking the
vicious cycle of poverty, illness and illiteracy constitutes an essential part
of the Bank's strategy to assist countries undergoing economic development.
The proposed project is, therefore, consistent with the Bank's Country
Assistance Strategy for Turkey approved by the Executive Directors on March
11, 1993.11/
1.29. Second, despite six consecutive Five-Year Plans, Turkey still spends too
little on low-cost, but highly effective, interventions such as maternal and
child care.  The construction of unnecessary hospitals, the purchase of

inappropriate tochnology, and poorly trained health care persomnel are
expensive and wasteful of public resources. The importance of approprlate
public policies to obtain better value for money spent on health care,
therefore, cannot be overstated, especially when the public budget Ls under
severe pressure. The lessons learned from implementation of the Flrst Eealth
Project, and experience gained elsewhere in the world, give the Bank a good
vantage point from which to support the NOR in making more appropriate
resource allocation choices.
1.30. Finally, many of the current deficiencies in the health sector must be
confronted at a very difficult time. The recent large fiscal deficits in
Turkey precipitated a severe financial crisis in 1993/94. This led the
Government to initiate a program of stabilization and reform measures in April
1994. The centerpiece of the program is fiscal adjustment, requiring a
substantial contraction in public spending. Assistance from the international
financial community has been sought through: (a) an IMF Standby Arrangement
approved in July 1994 to support the stabilization measures; and (b)
discussions with the Bank on a proposed Public Sector Adjustment Loan whlch
would cover the main structural elements contributing to the fiscal deficit.
Within the context of this fiscal crisis, the Government l ts continued to
attach a high priority to numan capital development. Witn funds not otherwLse
available, the Second Health Project will provide the financial resources for
critically needed investments in the health sector and help redirect health
expenditure towards more cost-effective areas of intervention.

II. TMB PROJECT
2.1. A Project Implementation Plan (PIP) was prepared during appraisal and
agreed with the NOR in accordance with procedures set out in the OP/BP/GB
10.00. 3W  Tha PIP appears as Annex 1 to this appraisal report.  The PIP is
based on findings of the appraisal mission and the Project Operational Manual
(PON) which was prepared by the PCU and NOR in accordance with terms-of-
reference provided by the Bank. The PON provides the detailed description of
how the Project will be implemented. A copy of the POM is available in the
project files. This chapter, therefore, provides only a summary description
of the project objectives and project activities. A detailed description for
each of these elements, therefore, normally presented in Chapter 2 of the
Staff Appraisal Report, can now be found in the recommended format for the PIP
presented in Annex 1.
A. Proiect Obiectives
2.2. Recognizing that widespread systemic reform in the health sector is a
long-term process, the Government and the World Bank have agreed that the
Second Health Project will continue to build upon the work and the lessons
learned during the First Health Project. The primary goal of the Second
Health Project is to reduce the burden of disease due to avoidable illness and
disability in Turkey. To achieve this goal, the specific objectives of the
Project are to: (a) improve equity of access to essential health services in
23 eastern, low-income, priority provinces (Map 3); and (b) improve the
quality of health care management in selected institutions.
B. Proiect Description
2.3. The proposed Project will provide fellowships, training, technical
assistance, studies, health programs, computer hardware and software, medical
and office furniture, equipment, vehicles, teaching materials, civil works and
Incremental recurrent costs (US$200.0 million equivalent total cost). It will
be implemented over a period of seven and a half years by NOH departments in
collaboration with provincial health directorates, health care units in the
project provinces, pilot hospitals and selected universities. The Project,
which covers 16 percent of Turkey's total population (11.8 million people in
1993) and 29 percent of its surface area in the 23 project provinces, consists
of the following two components:

2.4. The Primary Health Care component of the Project will improve equity In
access to essential health services by: (a) upgrading the training of primary
health care personnel; (b) expanding basic health care Interventions known to
be cost-effective; and (c) strengthening the network of health care facilities
to support the above activities. This component forms the centerpiece of the
Second Health Project. It sets priorities based on the leading causes of
mortality and morbidity, and it will keep these at the forefront of debates on
reforms in the health care system. The Primary Health Care Component consists
of the following three subcomponents: Primary Health Care Training, Basic
Health Care Interventions, and Infrastructure Development.
2.5. Primary Health Care Training (US$16.2 total cost). This subcomponent of
the Primary Health Care component will improve the quality of essential health
services by upgrading the training of primary health care personnel in the 23
project provinces. The Project will support: (a) curriculum dtvelopment,
practice-related learning materials and assistance in revising program
evaluation criteria; (b) training of trainers; (c) strengthening of in-service
training programs; (d) support In developing follow-up and supervision
mechanisms; and (e) opportunities for further training in primary health care
related areas.
2.6. Basic Health Care Interventions (USS2.9 million total cost). This
subcomponent of the Primary Health Care component will improve the
effectiveness of essential health services through: (a) extension of
successful interventions to the 23 project provinces (UNICEF-inspired Safe
Motherhood and Midwife Training Program, the Acute Respiratory Infectlon
Program, and Child Nutrition and Growth Monitoring Programs); (b) support for
public education campaigns (TV and advertising campaigns); (c) development and
implementation of a referral system; (d) support for community participation

(a) upgrading the training of staff in policymaking, planning, management,
monitoring and evaluation; (b) decentralizing and restructuring management
responsibility in six pilot hospitals;2V/ (c) introducing basic management

the project provinces as well as evaluating a more comprehensive management
information system in one urban pilot hospitl.l in western Turkey; and

project provinces, as well as selected NOR staff in Ankara; (c) providing a
rapid management development program tailored to the specific needs of the
pilot hospital managers; and (d) developing a strategy for extending this to
other parts of the country at a later point in time.

2.10. HolDital Nanagement Restructuring (US84.5 million total cost). This
subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will improve
performance of hospital services (cost-effectiveness, efficiency and quality)
by: (a) introducing a new organizational structure in six pilot hospitals;
(b) redefining the functions of senior managers in the pilot hospitals; and
(c) Increasing the responsibility, accountability and autonomy (including
financial) of managers in the pilot hospitals.
2.11. Comnute;ized Management SupDort Systems (USS12.4 million total cost).
This subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will provide
policymakers, managers, and health care providers with computerized management
support systems to improve the cost-effectiveness and efficiency of their
services. Thirty-four hospitals in the project provinces will receive small
network-based management support systems to perform basic hospital management
functions, such as personnel, payroll, accounting, general ledger, billing,
pharmacy, critical materials management, and admissions, discharge and
transfer functions. One urban hospital in Western Turkey will receive a
comprehensive network-based management support system.
2.12. Pre-investment Studies and Proiect Manaeement (USS16.8 million total
cost). This subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will
ensure that future reforms in the health sector focus adequately on reducing
the burden of illness due to preventable and treatable diseases or disability
in Turkey. A National Burden of Disease (NBD) study will be conducted,
applying the methodology developed for the 1993 World Development Report
(World Bank 1993). When combined with in-country cost-utility studies, it
will be possible to determine affordable cost-effective packages of health
interventions that will address a significant proportion of preventable and
treatable disease throughout the country (see Annex 4 for details).
2.13. This subcomponent of the Project will also conduct a limited number of
pilot studies to develop the Government's IHCR Program which could be
supported by a Third Health Project. It will continue providing technical
assistance and limited equipment to maintain an operational PCU in the MOg.
C. Environmental Impact
2.14. The Project has been classified as C: "No appreciable environmental
impact." Special attention will be given to appropriate disposal of sanitary,
medical and laboratory wastes. The Project is expected to have a beneficial
environmental impact through improved sanitation facilities for the disposal
of infectious wastes and other solid wastes in the rehabilitated health
centers and hospitals. Environmental considerations, especially concerning
the materials available locally for health center and hospital construction
and maintenance, will be factored into building quality standards and
procurement of building materials.

III. PROJECT COSTS. FINANCING. MANAGEMENT AND IHPLEMENTATION
A. Introduction
3.1.  This chapter provides only a_ summarv of project costs, financing,
management and implementation arrangements, procurement, disbursements, audit
reporting, and status of preparation. A detailed description for each of
these elements, normally presented in Chapter 3 of the Staff Appraisal Report
(SAR), can now be found in the recommended format for the PIP presented in
Annex 1.
B. Proiect Cost
3.2.    The total cost of the Second Health Project is estimated at about
TL9.8 trillion equivalent, including contingencies, taxes and duties. The
total base cost is estimated at US$126.9 million. Physical contingencies are
estimated at US$6.7 million. Price contingencies between negotiations
(August 1994) and the end of the seven and a half year project implementation
period will amount to about US$66.4 million or 33 percent of total cost. The
foreign exchange component is estimated at about US$53.9 million including
contingencies, or about 27 percen: of total project cost. Taxes and duties
are estimated at US$19.7 million equivalent.
3.3. The total project cost includes fellowships, training, technical
assistance, studies, community health programs, computer hardware and
software, medical and office furniture, equipment, vehicles, teaching and
public education materials, new construction, refurbishment of facilities and
Incremental recurrent costs incurred during project implementation. The
estimated cost distributed among project components is shown in Table 1 of
Annex 1. Project costs are summarized by expenditure category in Table 2 of
Annex 1. A breakdown of costs by component and expenditure category is found
in Table 3 of Annex 1. The project costs include about TL1.4 trillion or
uS$23.0 million to cover incremental operating costs directly attributable to
the Project during the seven and a half year implementation period.
C. Project Financing
3.4. The proposed Bank loan of US$150.0 million will finance 75 percent of
total project costs, comprising 100 percent of the foreign exchange cost
(US$53.9 million) of the Project and 76 percent of the local cost, excluding
taxes (US$96.1 million). The Government will finance the balance of project
costs (US$50.0 million) or about 24 percent of local costs (US$30.3 million),
excluding duties and taxes, which are estimated at US$19.7 million equivalent.
Incremental operating costs comprising building and equipment maintenance
services, annual maintenance costs of computers and software, and staff
salaries (US$23.0 million equivalent) will be financed entirely by the
Government. Averaged over the life of the Project, this represents three
percent of the projected 1995 budget allocation for the MOH of TL54.7
trillion.



- 16 -
3.5. To assist the NOR in recruiting specialized consultants and conducting
the surveys required for project preparation, a Project Preparation Pacillty
of US$1.5 million was made available by the Bank in April 1993. Repayment of
the Project Preparation Advance is included ln the project costs. In
addition, a grant of Yen122.3 million (about US$1.0 million) was awarded by
the Government of Japan to finance studies related to the design and
implementation of MOE's reform program which will pave the way for a Third
Health Project, focussiug on health care financing reforms.
3.6. The MOH han also requested retroactive financing of up to US$10 million
to cover expenditures incurred 12 months prior to project effectiveness to
support the following activities: (a) technical assistance to support
coordination of the primary health care training programs and coordination of
programs such as the Safe Motherhood and Midwifery Training Program;
(b) technical assistance from Turkish institution(s) to support implementation
of the health services management training program; (c) technical assistance
to support implementation of the management support systems subcomponent;
(d) technical assistance from Turkish institutions to support implementation
of a National Burden of Illness assessment and cost-utility studies in Turkey;
and (e) equipment for selected health posts for midwives, health centers, and
maternal and child centers (see Annex 1 for details on procurement
arrangements).
D. Project NanaRement and Imulementation
3.7.    The Project will be implemented by MOR departments, provincial health
directorates, health care units in the project provinces, pilot hospitals and
selected universities over a seven and a half-year implementation period.
3.8. A Project Coordination Committee (PCC), established and functioning well
under the First Health Project, will assume a broad oversight responsibility
for the Second Project. The Committee will provide guidance and advice on
project implementation and ensure that Project objectives remain consistent
with national health policy throughout the life of the Project. The PCC will
be chaired by the Undersecretary of the Ministry of Health and include the
department heads of the MOH General Directorates, the head of the Project
Coordination Unit (PCU), and representatives from the State Planning
Organization, and Treasury. Day-to-day coordination, management and
monitoring of all project-related activlties will be the responsibility of the
PCU.
3.9. The Project will require, on average, about 25 staff-weeks of Bank
supervision per year during the first two years of implementation to
successfully launch several critical project related activities (training
programs) and decrease to about 15 staff-weeks in the later years of
Implementation. Wherever passible, Bank staff will supervise the First and
Second Health Projects during the same mission, as well as initiating
preparation of the planned Third Health Project, thereby ensuring some economy
of effort in utilizing Bank staff resources.

R. Protect Procurement Arranuements
3.10. Items to be procured will be grouped into major packages to encourage
competitive bidding and permit bulk procurement. The standard procurement
table is provided in Annex 1, Table 7. Procurement will be carried out in
accordance with World Bank Guidelines. Major procurement categories include:
about US$92.5 million to be procured through international competitive bidding
(ICJ) procedures; about US$6.6 milllon through local competitive bidding
(LCB); US$1.7 million through international shopping; and US$2.8 million
through local shopping (LS).  Local procurement will be in acco: ance with
agreements reached between the Bank and the Turkish Government during the 1994
Country Procurement Assessment Review. It is expected that US$6.2 million
will be procured either directly from UNICEF or through IC3. It is also
expected that US$1.8 million will be procured through direct contracting
(books and intellectual property). Procurement packages, including estimated
cost and time of completion, are presented in the PIP (Annex 1).
3.11. Bank standard bidding documents for goods and works will be used for the
project. The PIP (Annex 1) contains a detailed list of equipment packages to
be procured under the Project. A description of the capacity of the
implementing agencies to carry out procurement procedures, as well as the
procurement monitoring process by the Borrower, is found in Annex 1.
P. Disbursements
3.12. The proposed Project is expected to be disbursed over a period of about
seven and a half years in accordance with the relevant profile for the Region,
based on the experLence of the Fir-t Health Project and recent improvements in
project implementation under coordination of the PCU. The closing date will
be December 31, 2001. A detailed disbursement plan is provided in the PIP,
Annex 1.
3.13.  To facilitate timely project implementatlon, the Government will
establish, maintain and operate, under terms and conditions acceptable to the
Bank, a Special Account denominated in a freely convertible currency. The
authorized allocation will be US$6.0 million. lowever, during the first year
of the Project, an initial amount limited to US$2.0 million will be deposlted.
When the aggregate amount of disbursement reaches US$25.0 million, the amount
deposited in the Special Account will be increased to the full authorized
allocation of US$6.0 million.
G.  Project Audit  Rorting and  luation
3.14. Project Accounts and the Special Account will be audited In accordance
with the Bank's "Guidelines for Financial Reporting and Auditing of Projects
Financed by the World Bank" (March 1982). Annex 1 shows the detailed plan for
Project Auditing.
3.15. The PCU will prepare semi-annual descriptive and financial reports on
each project component, objective an, actlvity (beginning from the date of
Loan Effectiveness). Annex 1 shows the detailed plan for Project reportitg
and annual reviews.

H. Status of PreDaration
3.16. The preparatory work and experlence gained by MOB and PCU staff under
the First Health Project have greatly facilitated preparation of the Second
Health Project. The scope of work required to prepare the Second Project
called for a clear distinction of responsibilities between the two operations.
New project preparation working subgroups were established from within the
existing PCU and NOR departments of the KOH. A detailed description of the
PCU and its organizational chart are included in Annex 1. A PON, which was
prepared as part of Project preparation, is included in the project files.
3.17. Desisnation of Prolect Coordination and Implementation Resoonsibilities.
Implementation and coordination responsibilities of the MOH implementation
units and the PCU have been defined. The principal staff of the PCU are in
place asd are supported by technical assistance through a Management Services
Agreement with the United Nations Development Program's Office of Project
Services (UNDP/OPS). Annex 1 Includes a detailed description bf the project
coordination and implementation responsibilities.



- 19 -
IV. BENEFITS AID RXSKS
A. Benefits
4.1 The Project's major benefit will be to reduce the number of avoidable
deaths and disability In the project provinces, especially among women and
children. Targeted investments in training and facilities will lead to better
staffed and equipped essential health services in these provinces. Such
interventions are well known to be cost-effective and a precondition for
improving maternal and child survival. A second benefit of the Project will
be to obtain better value for money spent on health services. Staff in the
project provinces will be trained in health services management and provided
with management support systems, alloving them to improve the quality of
services provided and avoid wasting scarce resources.
B. Risks
4.2 A major risk faced by the Project is that it may become difficult to
implement and supervise activities in a number of the project provinces which
currently have accessibility problems. Contingency plans are included to
allow: (a) out-of-province training where necessary; (b) pre-selection of only
urban facilities for civil works to ensure the possibility of an adequate
supervision, staffing and supply of equipment; (c) province-by-province
costing to allow flexibility under a phased implementation plan; and (d) use
of a professional engineering firm to oversee the construction programs. A
second risk is the limited institutional capacity of MOR departments,
especially in the area of training. This risk will be mitigated by:
(a) appropriate deployment of staff trained through fellowships financed under
the First Health Project; (b) technical assistance, including management
contracts for logistic support in coordinating training activities;
(c) decentralization of training efforts to provincial teams; (d) modular
distance-based education; (e) improved coordination of the different General
Directorates in an integrated approach to training activities; and
(f) collaboration with universities as co-participants in the implementation
of training progrums.



- 20 -
V. AGREEMESNT RACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
5.1. The following actions are required to assure successful implementation
of the Project and attainment of the broader project objectives.
5.2. During negotiations, assurances were provided that:
a. The Borrower shall, by June 30, 1998: (1) develop and initiate the
implementation of programs similar to the programs referred to under
Part A(2)(a) of the Project [Basic Health Care Interventions], to
improve the quality and effectiveness of, and access to, primary
health care in the Borrower's provinces other than the project
provinces; and (ii) ensure that disposal of medical materials and
waste and protection of X-ray facilities and staff are undertaken at
all times in conformity with the Borrower's applicable regulations
and in accordance with safety, health and environmental standards
satisfactory to the Bank (Loan Agreement, Section 4.02 (a) and (b)).
b. For the purpose of facilitating the efficient carrying out by MNO of
its activities under the Project, the Borrower shall maintain, with
membership, resources and terms of reference satisfactory to the
Bank, the units, teams and committees referred to in the Annex to
Schedule 5 of the Loan Agreement (Loan Agreement, Schedule 5(2)).
c. The Borrower shall: (i) ensure that the Project shall be carried out
in accordance with the requirements, and other details set forth in
an operations manual (the Project Operations Manual) agreed upon
between the Borrower and the Bank, as the same may be amonded wlth
the agreement of the Bank; (ii) employ consultants, with
qualifications and terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank, in
order to assist in the carrying out of the Project, including in the
management of the implementation thereof, the design and constructlon
supervision of the works thereunder and the procurement of goods,
works and services therefor; (iii) ensure (a) the adequate deployment
and continuity of primary health care personnel in the Project
Provinces and (b) the adequate equipping and supplying with drugs and
other consumables of the primary health care facilities located in
the said provinces, all in accordance with the requirements set forth
in the Project Operations Manual; and (iv) ensure that the training
activities under the Project shall be carried out in accordance with
programs, including time-schedules for the implementation thereof and
criteria for the selection of candidates thereunder, satisfactory to
the Bank. (Loan Agreement, Schedule 5(3)).
Recommedation
5.3. Subject to the above, the proposed operation provides a suitable basis
for a loan of US$150.0 million to the Government of Turkey.

REPUBLIC OF TURKEY
SECOND HEALTH PROJECT
ESSENTIAL HEALTH SERVICES AND MANAGEMENT DEVELOPMENT
IN EASTERN AND SOUTHEASTERN ANATOLIA
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION PLAN
Introduction
1.   The Project Implementation Plan (PIP) was prep-red during appraisal and
agreed with the Ministry of Health (MOH), in accordance with procedures set
out in the OP/BP/GB 10.00.1/ The PIP is based on findings of the appraisal
mission and the Project Operational Manual (POX) which was prepared by the
Project Coordination Unit (PCU) and MOH in accordance with terms-of-reference
provided by the Bank. The PON provides details on how the Project will be
implemented. A copy of the POX is available in the project files.
I. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Scope and Obiectives
2.    The primary goal of the Second Health Project is to reduce the burden of
disease due to avoidable illness and disability in Turkey. To achieve this
goal, the specific objectives of the Project are to: (a) improve equity of
access to essential health services in 23 eastern, low-income, priority
provinces (see Map 3); and (b) improve the quality of health care management
in selected institutions.
B. Proiect DescriDtion
3.   The proposed Project will provide fellowships, training, technical
assistance, studies, health programs, computer hardware and software, medical
and office furniture, equipment, vehicles, teaching materials, civil works and
incremental recurrent costs (US$200.0 million equivalent total cost). It will
be implemented over a period of seven and a half years by MOH departments in
collaboration with provincial health directorates, health care units in the
project provinces, pilot hospitals and through selected universities. The
Project, which covers 16 percent of Turkey's total population (nine million
people in 1990) and 29 percent of its surface area, consists of the following
two components:
CPR issued neo procedures on September 17, 1993 requiring that an Implementation Plan be prepared
and included in the Staff Appraisal Report. These procedures will be incorporated and issued in the
OP/BP/GB 10.00, Investment lending: Identification to Board Approval.
