-25 -                               Annex I
Primary Health Care Comoonent (USS1S5.6 millioo total cost}
4.    The Primary Health Care component of the Project will improve equity of
access to essential health services bys (a) upgrading the training of primary
health care personnel; (b) expanding basic health care interventions known to
be cost-effective; and (c) strengthening the network of health care facilities
to support the above activities. This component forms the centerpiece of the
Second Health Project. It sets priorities based on the leading causes of
mortality and morbidity, and it will keep these at the forefront of debates on
reforms in the health care system.
5.    The Primary Health Care Component consists of the following three
subcomponents: (a) Primary Health Care Training; (b) Basic Health Care
Interventions; and (c) Infrastructure Development. All construction,
equipment, furniture and vehicles needed under the other subcomponents of the
project have been costed under this subcomponent with the exception of the
computer hardware and software for the management support systems
subcomponent.
6.    Primary Realth Care Trainina (USS16.2 total cost).  This subcomponent of
the Primary Health Care component will improve the quality of essential health
services by upgrading the training of primary health care personnel in the 23
project provinces. It will strengthen short-term continuing education
programs at the provincial level, emphasizing cost-effective interventions,
practical skills in health promotion and disease prevention, health education,
priority health programs, quality assurance in primary health care and
teamwork approaches. The Project will provide for: (a) curriculum
development, practice-related learning materials and assistance in revising
program evaluation criteria; (b) training of trainers; (c) strengthening of
in-service training programs; (d) support in developing follow-up and
supervision mechanisms; and (e) opportunities for further training in primary
health care related areas. Community participation in health matters will be
stimulated through the training of community health volunteers and community
health councils. Primary and secondary school teachers will be trained in
health promotion.
7.    The training programs will be organized by the General Directorates of
MOH under the coordination and management of the PCU, in close collaboration
with Turkish universities, medical schools and secondary level vocational
schools of nursing and midwifery. A Primary Health Care Technical Committee,
with representation from line departments of the MON, has already been
established and will be responsible for overall development of the programs
(general strategies, curriculum development, supervision and evaluation).
Twenty-seven multi-professional Provincial Training Teams with seven members
each (four of the 23 project provin.ces will have two training teams), with
support from three Regional Training Teams to facilitate coordination among
the various training activities, will form the nucleus for most training
activities. A phased approach will be used to disseminate information about
health programs from the central level through provincial teams to the
population. The Central Training Committee will work closely with the
Regional Training Teams in giving support to the Provincial Training Team



- 26 -                              Ann= I
during the initial induction phase. Trained clinical staff in the health
centers (doctors, nurses and midwives) will assist in the subsequent training
of community councils and community health volunteers within their catchment
areas and villages. These community resources will then participate in health
education of the local population. Each of the three Regional and 27
Provincial Training Teams will be responsible for training approximately 70
doctors, 60 nurses, 90 midwives, six technicians and eight other primary
health care workers annually. Each province will also train two six-member
community health council groups and 350 community volunteers during the
project lifetime.
8.    The Project will support a modular approach to training which will focus
on the main health problems facing the population in the project provinces.
Modules will be produced by the various Training Teams for the following
programs: (a) family health, including maternal and child health, nutrition,
and family planning; (b) environmental health, including water, waste,
personal, animal, air and food hygiene, and potential for tropical and water
borne diseases; and (c) common health problems such as tuberculosis, other
respiratory and infectious diseases, and accidents. The modules will be
developed with different options for depth of content according to the needs
of the target group of health personnel (doctors, nurses, midwives,
technicians and others) and local needs of the population. Training programs
for the community councils and community volunteers will also follow a modular
plan. Targeted health professionals will receive additional job- or function-
specific training through the training program. Distance-based educational
programs will be prepared to reach out to personnel in remote regions. These
will be followed up by on-site discussions and practice with provincial
training teams. hanagement training for the personnel staffing essential
health services will be provided through the Health Services Management
Training subcomponent of the Project. Given the large number of short-term
courses, an external firm will be hired to assist in coordinating the
administrative aspects of the programs. The same firm will provide management
support in coordinating activities under the Basic Health Care Interventions
subcomponent.
9.    The Project will provide technical assistance (60 staff-months for
policy development, 362 staff-months for management support and 16 staff-
months for institutional capacity building), fellowships (30 staff-years),
local and international study tours, training, teaching materials, audio-
visual equipment, hardware, software, library resources, and training for
primary health care workers to deal effectively with the key threats to good
health in the project provinces. The civil works, medical equipment and
vehicles required for the new training programs have been costed under the
Infrastructure Development subcomponent.
10.   Basic Health Care Interventions (USS2.9 million total cost).  This
subcomponent of the Primary Health Care component will improve the
effectiveness of essential health services through: (a) extension of
successful interventions to the 23 project provinces; (b) support for public
education campaigns; (c) development and implementation of a referral system;
(d) support for community participation and involvement by NGOs in the health



- 27 -                             Annex 1
sector; and (e) evaluation of cost-effective interventions through operational
research. The Provincial Health Directorates and health care units will
expand known programs with demonstrated success in other parts of the world
such as the UNICEF-MOH Safe Motherhood and Midwife Training Program, the Acute
Respiratory Infection (ARI) Program, and Child Nutrition and Growth Monitoring
Programs. They will increase existing public education programs through mass
media (TV and advertising campaigns) to focus on maternal and child health,
positive actions which can be taken to reduce avoidable mortality and
morbidity, and the appropriate use of health services. The Project will also
improve communication and information flows through the different levels of
the health care system by developing and analyzing a referral system under
controlled conditions. Successful experiences will be extended to other parts
of the country. The participation of NGOs will be encouraged through grants
to support new initiatives in community participation in the health sector.
Finally, in close coordination with the National Burden of Disease Assessment
(see description under Pre-investment Studies and Annex 4), the Project will
assess the cost-effectiveness of different types of basic health care
interventions through operational research in the project provinces.
11.   The Project will provide technical assistance (34 staff-months of policy
development and 153 staff-months for institutional capacity building),
training, and studies to develop and implement the basic health care
intervention programs. The civil works, medical equipment and vehicles
required for the basic health services interventions have been costed under
the Infrastructure Development subcomponent.
12.   Infrastructure Develounent (USS136.5 million total costl.3] This
subcomponent of the Primary Health Care component will improve access to
essential health services in the 23 project provinces through: (a) the
refurbishment and upgrading of existing facilities, and the construction of a
limited number of new facilities (49); and (b) procurement of: (i) equipment;
(Li) furniture; and (iii) vehicles. The civil works will focus primarily on
the construction, furnishing and equipping of urban in-service clinical
training centers (29), urbam health centers (14), public health laboratories
(3) and cold storages for vaccines (3). The Project will also include the
renovation, furnishing and equipping of four mid-sized hospitals with a 200-
300 bed capacity, urban health centers (110), rural health centers (115) and
public health laboratories (4). This subcomponent will provide the necessary
physical infrastructure and clinical facilities to promote, in a balanced
manner in designated areas, the primary health care strategy developed under
the Project.
13.   To improve the quality of ante- and post-natal care, the Project will
provide health posts for midwives with weighing scales (baby and adult), small
refrigerators and an examining table. Midwives in the primary care setting
(health posts for midwives, health centers and maternal and child centers)
All comtruction, equipmet, furniture and vehicLes needed uider the other suboaponts of the
project have been costed uwder this subcomponent with the exception of the compater hardwre and
software for the management support systems subcomponent.



- 28 -                            Annex I
will be provided with "midwife kits" and disposable refills to rGplenish these
as needed on a regular basis to improve the quality of assisted births. To
broaden the scope and cost-effectiveness of essential health services, each
health center and maternal and child center will receive a set of basic
diagnostic equipment, including a centrifuge to prepare urinalysis specimens,
two hemoglobin meters, two glucometers, weighing scales (baby and adult) and a
refrigerator to store vaccines. The 27 urban in-service clinical training
centers will also be equipped with basic ambulatory diagnostic equipment,
including a basic fixed X-Ray machine, blood cell counter, bacteriology
equipment and autoclave. To strengthen the basic health care referral network
in each of the project provinces, each state hospital, four obstetrics
hospitals and seven of the other large non-state hospitals will each be
equipped with basic diagnostic equipment (a fixed X-Ray, two mobile X-Rays, an
autoanalyser and pulse oximeter) and essential acute care equipment (two
portable monitor/defibrillators, an ECG machine, a respirator, an anesthesia
machine, a volumetric respirator, two nebulizers and incubators). The
obstetrics units will be provided with a fetal monitor, an ultrasound machine
and incubators. The Project will also provide 244 4-wheel drive vehicles to
improve transport and outreach activities.
14.   The Project will provide surveys of existing facilities, topographical
site surveys, soil tests, design of buildings, civil works, supervision of
construction and procurement of equipment and vehicles to strengthen essential
health services and their referral network in the project provinces. It will
provide on-the-spot training to strengthen the operation and utilization of
the new equipment.
Health PolicX and Management Component (USS44.4 million total cost)
15.   The Health Policy and Management component of the Project will improve
the quality of health care management in selected institutions byt
(a) upgrading the training of staff in policymaking, planning, management,
monitoring and evaluation; (b) decentralizing and restructuring management
responsibility in six pilot hospitals;VJ (c) introducing basic management
support systems in Provincial Health Directorates and selected hospitals in
the project provinces as well as evaluating a more comprehensive management
information system in one urban pilot hospital in western Turkey; and
(d) conducting pre-investment studies to support a broader reform of the
health sector. Activities supported under (b) and (c) are explicitly designed
as trial runs for later country-wide implementation.
16.   The Health Policy and Management component will consist of the following
four subcomponents: (a) Health Services Management Training; (b) Hospital
Management Restructuring; (c) Computerized Management Support Systems; and
(d) Pre-investment Studies and Project Management. Any civil works, medical
equipment and vehicles needed to implement subcomponents (a), (b) and (c) have
Two of the six pitot hospitals for the Hospital Management Restructuring subcomponent which uere
started under the First Health Project (Bilecik and Eskisehir), are outside the 23 project
provinces. The other four pilot hospitaLs under this subcomponent are the sawe as the four
hospitals that witl be renovated under the Infrastructure development subcomponent.



- 29 -                              Annex
been costed under the Infrastructure Development subcomponent. The computer
hardware and software needed under subcomponent (c) have been costed under
subcomponent (c), not under the Infrastructure Development subcomponent.
17.   Health Service. Management Trainina (USS10.7 million total costi.  This
subcomponent will strengthen the management capabilities of the health care
system by: (a) training a critical mass of Turkish trainers in the concepts
and methodology of modern health services management; (b) developing a
continuing education program to upgrade the managerial skills of hospital
managers, staff in health centers, and provincial Health Directors in the
project provinces, as well as selected KOH staff in Ankaral (c) providing a
rapid management development program tailored to the specific needs of the
pilot hospital managers; and (d) developing a strategy for extending this to
other parts of the country at a later time. The subcomponent will be
delivered by a partnership between: (a) a Turkish university (or a consortium
of two Turkish universities with at least one from the 23 project provinces)
which will provide the institutional framework for the trainers; and (b) a
suitably qualified foreign institution or consortium of institutions which
will provide the technical assistance. Turkish and foreian institutions will
train 26 trainers in the first year (20 of the trainers w_ll be retrained in
the fifth year of the Project). In the project provinces, these trainers will
subsequently be responsible for training 192 hospital staff, 288 people from
provincial health directorates and 120 people from the central KOH over the
seven and a half year Project cycle. They will also train 60 people from the
six pilot hospitals. In addition, 140 health center doctors and 100
specialists from the project provinces will be trained annually throughout the
Project cycle.
18.   The "Training the Trainers Program", which wilL provide the basis for
all subsequent management training activities, requires an early selection by
the partnership institutions of potential candidates for the program. Once
completed, this program will be followed by establishment of the phased
management training program described above. The management training needs of
the pilot hospitals will be met, in the initial phase of the project, through
an intensive eight week course provided in Turkish by the newly trained
trainers with support from foreign technical assistance. The participants
will then take part in a brief overseas study tour to observe the functioning
of decentralized hospitals and health services. Following the first cycle of
the basic management training program, more advanced continuing management
training programs will be offered at repeated intervals throughout the life of
the Project.
19.   The modular contents of the continuing management training programs will
vary according to the needs of the various participants. The range of
modules, however, will include introduction to: running health services;
health policy and planning; strategic management; management of change;
financial management and planning; using information for management; human
resource management; interpersonal skills and time management; epidemiology
for management; and managing quality assurance. The programs will have a
particular orientation towards improving the functioning of the essential
health service network, the appropriate referral system to secondary care and



- 30 -                              Annex I
the efficient operation of the hospital sector.   All the programs will be
designed to have immediate, practical benefit to the health service. The
Project will also strengthen the Masters level programs in health services
management at selected universities to meet the country's long-term needs.
Faculty and professional development will be supported through postgraduate
fellowships abroad (Masters and PhD degrees).
20.   The Project will provide technical assistance (four staff-months for
policy development, 63 staff-months for management support and 524 staff-
months for institutional capacity building), fellowships (58 staff-years),
local and international study tours, training, teaching materials, audio-
visual equipment, hardware, software and library resources to establish and
strengthen the described programs. The limited civil works required for the
now training programs have been costed under the Infrastructure Development
subcomponent.
21.   Hospital Manaaement Restructurina (USS4.5 million total cost).  This
subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will improve
performance of hospital services (cost-effectiveness, efficiency and quality)
by: (a) introducing a new organizational structure in six pilot hospitals;
(b) redefining the functions of senior managers in the pilot hospitals; and
(c) increasing the responsibility, accountability and autonomy (including
financial) of managers in the pilot hospitals. The Project will supports
(a) the recruitment, selection and development of hospital management teams;
(b) the establishment of departments responsible for business planning,
general management, finance, personnel, clinical affairs, and in-service
training; (c) the development and introduction of hospital policies and
procedures to support an increased autonomy of the hospitals and improved
referral systems; (d) the installation of computerized management support
systems to provide the information that is needed for rational decision
making; (e) the development of accounting systems, costing and pricing
mechanisms to improve efficiency in the use of resources (equipment,
materials, staff and services); and (f) the development of quality standards
for the improvement of patient care and the environment.
22.   Training in health services management and computerized information
systems will be provided under the Health Services Management Training and the
Computerized Management Support Systems subcomponents, respectively. The
Project will support macro planning exercises within the pilot hospitals as
guidance to managers in developing future-oriented business plans for their
institutions. Emphasis will be placed on developing an ability in strategic
planning, management of change, decision making, conflict management,
leadership development, financial management, accounting, monitoring and use
of information, communication and dealing with the external environment as
applied to the management of a health provider institution.
23.   The Project will provide technical assistance (29 staff-months for
policy development and 192 staff-months for institutional capacity building),
fellowships (72 staff-years), local and international study tours, training,
hardware, software, equipment, development of studies and interventions
designed to facilitate a decentralization and restructuring of management
responsibility within the health sector to the regional and local hospital
levels. The limited civil works required for hospital restructuring have been
costed under the Infrastructure Development subcomponent.



- 31 -                              Anane  1
24. QomputSriged Management Support Systems (USS12.4 million total costs.
This subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will provide
policymakers, managers, and health care providers with computerized management
support systems to improve the cost-effectiveness and efficiency of their
services. Thirty-four hospitals in the project provinces will receive small
network-based management support systems to perform basic hospital management
functions, such as personnel, payroll, accounting, general ledger, billing,
pharmacy, critical materials management, and admIssions, discharge and
transfer functions. one urban hospital in Western Turkey will receive a
comprehensive network-based management support system to perform and
integrates (a) financial and administrative management; and (b) clinical
functions, such as radiology, hematology, obstetrics, etc. In addition, the
comprehensive system will provide access to clinical records information.
Under the First aealth Project, a 15-module health information system (HIS) is
being developed for the central MOB and provincial Health Directorates.
Following successful deployment under the First Project, the Second Health
Project will extend a basic health information system to the Provincial
Directorates of the 23 project provinces. These systems will enhance overall
health care resource management, service delivery, and statistics collection.
Deployment of the basic and comprehensive hospital systems and the provincial
Health Directorate systems will all be tightly coupled, in terms of both
timing and substance, to training of relevant managers and staff.
25.   Hospital managers and staff will be trained in the concepts and the
specific functions provided by the computerized support systems being
implemented to: (a) gather accurate and up-to-date information on reasons for
visits to health facilities; (b) provide health services outputs, and track
expenditures; (c) identify ways to contain costs; and (d) operate their
services effectively and efficiently. Clinicians wiLl be trained to use the
system to identify cost-effective treatments by combining financial data with
information from the admission/discharge information system. Clerical staff
will be trained in data coding, transfers from paper to electronic forms,
aggregation of data from local to national levels, and analysis and
interpretation of data. This training will be provided under the Health
Services Management Training subcomponent of the Project.
26.   The Project will provide technical assistance (35 staff-months for
policy development and 350 staff-months for institutional capacity building),
study tours, and training to support development and implementation of the
application software. The Project will provide the necessary hardware,
software and office equipment. The limited civil works that will be required
to accommodate the new management support systems have been costed under the
Infrastructure Development subcomponent.
27.  Pro-investment Studies and Proiect Manacement (uSS16.8 million total
cost). This subcomponent of the Health Policy and Management component will
ensure that future reforms in the health sector focus adequately on reducing
the burden of illness due to preventable and treatable diseases and disability
in Turkey. A National Burden of Disease (NBD) study will be conducted,
applying the methodology developed for the 1993 World Development Report
(World Bank 1993). This methodology combines mortality and morbidity into a
single internationally comparable unit, the Disability Adjusted Life Year
(DALY). The purpose of the study will be to determine the total burden of
disease in Turkey broken down by age, sex and disease category according to
ICD-10 diagnostic codes- The study will assist the Ministry of Health in



- 32 -                             Anne  1
determining current health priorities and predicting potential trends into the
next century. Ar attempt will be made to conduct a subregional breakdown of
some of the indicators. When combined with in-country cost-utility studies, it
will be possible to determine affordable cost-effective packages of health
interventions that will address a significant proportion of preventable and
treatable disease throughout the country. The Project will develop the
capacity of institutions in Turkey to undertake such a study through technical
assistance and fellowships, supporting at least two study cycles at a five
year interval during the life of the Project. The selected institution(s)
will conduct the study nationwide, not just in the 23 project provinces (see
Annex 4 for details).
28.   This subcomponent of the Project will also conduct a limited number of
pilot studies to develop the Government's IHCR Program which could be
supported by a Third Health Project. It will continue providing technical
assistance and limited equipment to maintain an operational PCU in the MOH.
29.   The Project will provide technical assistance (110 staff-months for
project management support and 1,512 staff-months for institutional capacity
building), fellowships (15 staff-years), international study tours and
training in the form of pilot projects, feasibility studies and policy advice.
Most of the feasibility studies will involve lump sum technical assistance
contracts to support policy development. It will provide the necessary
hardware, software and equipment for the studies to be conducted in an
effective and efficient manner, and to arssist in restructuring the NOH.
30.  a summary of the Project goals, objectives and activities is shown in
Chart 1 above.

34.   Bases of Coat Estimates.  Project costs were estimated as follows:
(a)   Base  Costs.  Cost estimates are based on a review of actual costs
incurred in other projects, such as the F1irst Health Project
(Loan 3057-TU) and the Employment and Training Project
(Loan 3541-TU). Additional information was derived from:
(a) costs of consultant services, fellowships and training based
on the rates currently charged by local, European and North
American consultants and training programs; (b) recent quotations
obtained from suppliers of hospital/medical and computer



- 35 -                             Annex I
equipment; (c) applications software costs from comparisons with
ongoing software development activities and estimated requirements
for technical books and journals; furniture costs are based on
market prices; (d) building and refurbishment costs from
consultants' surveys of the current costs for these items;
(e) funds allocated for the preparation of studies, estimated on
the basis of the aggregated costs of local and foreign specialized
services, unit costs for internal travel and subsistence, and
mission estimates of material and logistic needs; (f) staff-unit
costs from current public service salary scales with some
adjustments for the remuneration of local technical staff to a
level deemed competitive with the market for specialized services;
and (9) non-salary recurrent costs (materials and rent) based on
current Government rates. All project costs have been estimated
in US dollars on the basis of August 1994 prices at the official
exchange rate of TL31,180 per US dollar.94/
(b)   Contingencv Allowances.  Project costs include a contingency for
unforeseen physical variations (US$6.7 million) equivalent to five
percent of the base cost of civil works, goods, technical
assistance, training and fellowships, and ten percent for all
other project items. There is no physical contingency for staff
salaries. Price contingencies between negotiations (August, 1994)
and the end of the seven and a half year project implementation
period will amount to about US$66.4 million, or 33 percent of
total cost, and reflect Bank estimates of local and international
price increases.J/ Local price contingencies for FY95 are
particularly high, reflecting Turkey's recent economic situation.
in estimating the price contingencies, it is assumed that any
differentials between domestic and international inflation rates
will be offset by appropriate adjustments in exchange rates.
(c)   Foreion Exchanae Component.  The foreign exchange component is
estimated at about US$53.9 million including contingencies, or 27
percent of total project cost. Calculations of the foreign
exchange component were derived from an item-by-item analysis
resulting in the following: S percent for civil works; 75 percent
for medical and office equipment, computer hardware and software,
books and journals; 5 percent for furniture; 75 percent for
vehicles; 20 percent for training; 100 percent for foreign
fellowships; and 100 percent for foreign technical assistance.
The foreign exchange component in incremental recurrent
expenditure was estimated at 35 percent for the operation and
maintenance of computer hardware and software; 35 percent for
vehicles and other equipment maintenance; and S percent for
buildings maintenance.
W     Exchange rate assumptions for life of the Project: 34716 in FY95; 43156 in FY96; 50060 in FY 97;
55594 In FY98; 61783 In FY99; 68637 in FY 2000; 76436 in FY2001.
Fhreign inflation rates: 2.2 percent per year for the life of the Project. Loca l nfLation rates:
72.5 percent In FY95; 27.5 percent in FY96; 15.6 percent in FY9.7; 12.9 percent in FY98; and 11.7
percent in FY99-2001.



- 36 -                             Anne  1
(d)   Customs. Duties and Taxes.  The MOH is expected to be responsible for
all import duties on goods to be purchased under the Project.
Therefore, project costs include an estimated US$19.7 million equivalent
in direct and indirect taxes and duties on civil works, goods and
services, to be financed by the Government.
35.   Incremental Recurrent Costs.  The Project costs include TLI.4 trillion
or US$23.0 million to cover incremental operating costs directly attributable
to the Project during the seven-year period. These consist of salaries of
additional staff, and incremental operation and maintenance expenditure for
equipment, vehicles and buildings. Averaged over seven years, the yearly
requirement represents three percent of the projected 1995 budget allocation
for the MOH of TL54.7 trillion. Measures now being instituted in the health
sector to reduce recurrent costs, improve efficiency and increase collection
of user charges will place the incremental recurrent cost within sustainable
limits. During implementation, MOH will pay extra attention to monitoring the
adequacy of recurrent funds for maintenance and operation of facilities ane
newly instituted programs, and take the necessary actions to rectify any
deficiencies.
D.  Detailed Financino Plan
36.   The proposed Bank loan of US$150.0 million would finance 100 percent of
the foreign exchange cost (US553.9 million) of the Project and 76 percent of
the local cost, excluding taxes (US$96.1 million). The Government would
finance the balance of project costs (US$S50.0 million), or about 24 percent of
local costs (US$30.3 million) excluding duties and taxes, which are estimated
at US$19.7 million equivalent. Recurrent incremental costs comprising
building and equipment maintenance services, annual maintenance costs of
computers and software, staff salaries and other non-salary operational costs
(US$23.0 million equivalent) would be financed entirely by the Government.
The State Planning Organization (SPO) has formally approved the Project in the
investment program.

II. IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
A. Oraanizations Responsible for the Proiect
38.   The Borrower is the Republic of Turkey which is represented by the
Undersecretariat of Treasury and Foreign Trade. The Ministry of Health (XOB)
will have overall responsibility for the Project. (See Chart 2 for
organizational structure of MOH and PCU below).

39.   The Project Coordination Committee (PCC), which was established within
the MOM under the First Health Project, will also oversee the Second Health
Project. The Committee will provide guidance and advice on project
impl.mentation and ensure that the project objectives remain consistent with
national health policy throughout the life of the Project. The PCC will be
chaired by the Undersecretary of the MOH and will include the department heads



- 38 -                             Annex I
from the MOB General Directorates and the Project Coordination Unit (PCU), as
well as representatives from the State Planning Organization and Treasury.
The Undersecretary, as Chief Operating Officer of MOH, will have full
responsibility for coordinating activities of the General Directorates. Day-
to-day coordination, management and monitoring of all project-related
activities will be the responsibility of the PCU which will coordinate
activities of both the First and Second Health Projects.
B. acreements Between Borrower and ImDlementation Acencies
40. MOH will have overall responsibility for the Project. The coordination,
management and monitoring responsibility for the project activities shall lie
with the PCU. The relevant project activities will be undertaken in the
project provinces, pilot hospitals and selected universities. The following
departments in the MOH will have special roles in many of the project-related
activitiess General Directorates for Curative Services (CS), Maternal and
Child Care/Family Planning (MCH/FP), Primary Health Care (PHC), Health
Education (HE), and the Research, Planning and Coordination Council (RPCC).
Special contractual arrangements will be established between MOH, selected
universities and consulting firms by the PCU.
C. Responsibilities of the Imolementina Acencies
41. Protcot Coordination Unit. Overall responsibility for day-to-day
coordination, management and monitoring of the Project will rest with Ministry
of Health's PCU. The PCU is headed by a full-time Project Coordinator who
reports directly to the Undersecretary or delegated Deputy Undersecretary.
The Project Coordinator, who is a senior civil servant, is assisted by deputy
coordinators and senior advisors. The unit is staffed with health specialists
and personnel with skills in accounting, procurement, contract monitoring,
information processing, records management, and health planning and
management. The PCU is supported by technical staff from both the NOR and
external consultants provided under the UNDP/OPS Management Services
Agreement.
42. Responsibility for the actual implementation will rest with the
implementing agencies described in Section B above. In specific areas such as
procurement of technical assistance, contracting with external consultants and
designing major procurement packages for civil works and equipment, the PCU
will be directly involved. The PCU has had considerable experience under the
First Health Project in using Bank procurement procedure. The PCU's major
responsibility will be to coordinate, manage and monitor the Project according
to the agreed POM.
43. The specific responsibilities of the PCU are the following:
(a)   ensure that financial resources required under the Project are
made available to the implementing agencies in a timely manner.



- 39 -                              Annex I
The PCU will ensure the movement of official documents and
contracts through the usual approval processes and undertake
measures to expedite their release;
(b)   monitor the availability of Government funds by consolidating
estimates of budgetary needs (investment and recurrent) of the
Project (prepared jointly with the implementing agencies) for the
upcoming financial year, sufficiently ahead of schedule to obtain
the comments and concurrence of the interested agencies, including
the Bank, prior to submission for Government/Parliamentary
approval;
(c)   monitor project expenditures and costs (local and foreign),
process loan disbursement applications in collaboration with the
Central Bank of Turkey, and track disbursements of the Bank loan
and Government funds;
(d)   ensure compliance with the Bank Procurement Guidelines in the
acquisition of equipment and software packages, administration of
suppliers' and technical assistance contracts, as well as
fellowship and training plans for all MOH departments and
institutions, including higher levels of management involved :-n
project implementation;
(e)   consolidate requests and procure goods, services, and works in
accordance with World Bank Guidelines;
(f)   prepare and distribute consolidated periodic reports to the
relevant government and other participating institutions,
including the Bank, reflecting: (i) the status of implementation
progress, problems encountered and corrective actions needed; and
(ii) current costs of each project component and estimated costs
of completion;
(g)   ensure the timely preparation and submission to relevant
government institutions and the Bank of annual audit reports of
project expenditure, including Statements of Expenditure (SOEs)
and accounts;
(h)   liaise with the Bank and other involved agencies for project
administration and supervision matters;
(i)   ensure the placement and performance of staff receiving training
in Turkey and abroad, and of the consultants hired under the
Project; and
(j)   prepare the Project Completion Report (PCR) within six months
after the completion of the Project.



- 40-                               Annex 1
44.   MOH Department and other Imolementina Units.  The following MOH
departments and units will have responsibility for specific aspects of
implementation under the management and coordination of the PCU: The MCR/VP is
mainly responsible for maternal and child services as well as the delivery and
supervision of family planning services. The PHC is responsible for the
effective integration of all primary health care activities in public health
facilities, including rural health posts and urban health centers. Finally,
the HE is responsible for coordination of educational related activities and
vocational training programs. The MCH/FP, PHC and HE will be responsible for
implementation of the Primary Health Care Component. Curative Services
Directorate is responsible for operating the MOH hospitals and will be
responsible for activities relating to the Health Policy and Management
Component of the Project, with the exception of the Project Management and
Pre-Investment Studies sub-component.
45.   In addition, the Provincial Health Directorates are responsible for
implementing the health programs and overseeing general health activities at
the provincial level. In close collaboration with individual health care
units (rural health posts, health centers and hospitals), they will be
responsible for the detailed implementation of the various project related
activities.
46.   Each implementing agency will establish a mechanism with the PCU to
ensure the timely flow of resources for implementing its programs. Typically,
each implementing agency, under the coordination, management and monitoring of
the PCU, will be responsible for the following activities:
(a)   improving and updating TORS (the POX includes draft TORe for all
major technical assistance assignments), short-lists, and
contributing to technical evaluation of TA proposals received;
(b)   contributing to the preparation of detailed specifications and
procurement documents for approved equipment lists, contributing
to the review of bid packages and evaluation of offers receivedt
(c)   monitoring of detailed procedures, implementation schedules, and
program(s);
(d)   reviewing physical targets and preparation of revised estimates of
financial resources needed to support future actions; and
(e)   collecting and disseminating information, and preparation of
reports on implenantation of the relevant subcomponent.
47.  The following Chart shows the implementing agency/department responsible
for each subcomponent of the Project under the coordination, management and
monitoring of the PCU.

48.   The specific responsibilities of each implementing agency/department,
under the coordination, management and monitoring of PCU are as follows:
COMPONENT 1: PRIMARY iALTH CARE
(a)   Primary Health Care Training.  Responsibility for implementation
of this subcomponent rests with MCH/FP, PHC, and HE.
Implementation of this subcomponent will be carried out by
selected institutions, provincial health directorates, individual
health care units, and the var_..ous training teams which have
already been established (the Central Training Team, the three
Regional Training Teams, and the 29 Provincial Training Teams).
Specific objectives of the subcomponent include the following:
(i) institutionalizing a sustainable continuing education program
in PHC; (ii) implementing an integral approach to training
activities; (iii) improving community participation in health
matters; and (iv) strengthening PHC teaching in Health Vocational
Institutions. These objectives will be met by strengthening the
institutional capacity of the Ministry through recruitment of
consultants to support the development and implementation of the
training programs, training of trainers, faculty devolopment, a
fellowship program, teaching materials, audio visual equipment,
hardware, and the required software. The CDs will analyze and
apply lessons learned in the training related to successful
programs. A management consulting firm will be selected by MCH/FP
in accordance with Bank procedures to provide logistical support
to the implementing agencies.
(b)   Basic Health Care Interventions.  Implementation responsibility
for this subcomponent will rest with MCH/FP, PHC and CS. The



- 42 -                             Annexu 1
Provincial Health Directorates and individual health care units
will be responsible for implementing the following elementss (i)
public education; (ii) community-based activities; (iii) Lmproving
referral systems; and (iv) operational research. They will also
work closely with selected Universities, NOos, WHO, UNICEF, UNFPA,
and other related institutions. To insure successful
implementation of this subcomponent, the Provincial Health
Directorates and health care units will rece!.ve support from the
same management consulting firm recruited to support efforts under
the Primary Health Care Training subcomponent.
(c)  Infrastructure Pevelogmmnt.  Responsibility for implementing thli
subcomponent will be with the civil works and procurement division
of the PCU, in close collaboration with related departments of NOR
and recipient Provincial Health Directorates and health care
units. The PCU will be responsible for: (i) preparing TORs; (ii)
recruiting design and supervision consultants following procedures
acceptable to the Bank, for the construction and renovation of
hospitals, health care centers, and training centers; and (iii)
managing the new construction program for all health facilities.
Remodelling and upgrading of existing facilities will be done
under the responsibility of local authorities. Agreement has been
reached on the architectural designs for all health facilities to
be built under the Project. The process of selection and
acquisition of sites has been completed. The PCU will also be
responsible for coordinating and monitoring the procurement of
equipment, vehicles, and furniture. In carrying out these
activities, the PCU will work closely with the Research, Planning,
and Coordination Council of the MOH (division within MOH
responsible for construction and procurement), with a view to
permit it to take over most procurement activities and civil works
in the future.
COMPONENT 2: HEALTH POLICY AND MANAGEMENT
(a)   Health Services Manaaement Trainino.  Responsibility for
implementation of this subcomponent will rest with Curative
Services and Health Education General Directorates. The
development work and implementation of activities under this
subcomponent will be subcontracted through competitive bidding to
a Turkish university or a consortium of universities. The
university would be Turkish in order to ensure boths (i) the
necessary in-country institution building; and (ii) the ability to
design and deliver the proposed training programs in Turkish. The
Turkish university will be supported by a recognized foreign
consultancy institution which will be selected by MOH in
accordance with Bank procedures. The selected universities will
be responsible for: (i) study tours and workshops for management
teams of the pilot hospitals; (ii) training of trainers; (iii)
health services management training programs for staff in the MON,
Provincial Directorates, hospitals and individual health care



- 43-                              Annex 
units; and (iv) extension of the program to other parts of the
country.
(b)   Hosoital Management Restructurina.  Responsibility for
implementation of this subcomponent rests with the Curative
Services. The six selected Pilot Hospitals will be responsible
for implementation. They will be responsible for the development
of hospital financial management systems, strategic and business
plans, improved personnel management systems, and the
establishment of resource and quality assurance systems.
(c)   Comouterized Manaaement Suiooort Svstem.   Responsibility for
implementation of this subcomponent rests with the Management
Information Systems (MIS) Division of the PCU. This Division will
be responsible for: (i) the basic management support system
deployed to hospitals in the project provinces and an extension of
the ROH's Health Information System to the Directorates of these
provinces; and (ii) management of one hospital selected as the
test site for a more comprehensive MIS. The development work and
implementation of the activities under this subcomponent will be
obtained primarily from local and international consulting firms
and contractors, procured in accordance with Bank procedures.
Consistent with the national objective of developing the
informatice sector in Turkey, essentially all work will be
undertaken by the private sector.
(d)   Pre-investment Studies and Proiect Management Sunoort. The PCU
will have overall responsibility for recruiting consultants to
carry out the agreed preinvestment studies. Draft TORs have
already been prepared by the PCU and MOH for all the proposed
assignments. Consultants will be selected in accordance with Bank
Guidelines. The PCU will also be responsible for identifying a
suitable university or institution to carry out a National Burden
of Disease (NBD) study which will assess mortality and morbidity
patterns in Turkey, developing a rational frame of reference for
future policy directions in the health sector. A national
institution or consortia of universities will be contracted on a
competitive basis to conduct the study with assistance from a
foreign university with experience in this field. To support the
management of the PCU, the KOH may obtain administrative and
logistical support in form of "Management Support Services" either
by: (a) extending the existing Management Services Agreement (NSA)
between the MOH and the UNDP/OPS or; (b) through new contracts
with national and/or international institutions. The MSA was
originally awarded to UNDP/OPS at the beginning of the First
Health Project and was recently extended for that project.
49.   Pr2oiect operational Manual.  For each of the seven subcomponents of the
Project, the POX which has been prepared by the KOH and PCU describes the
following: (a) general/sector background (rationale for project
subcomponent); (b) key issues/problems (past, transitional, sector specific);
and (c) strategy for the proposed intervention: (i) objectives of
intervention (broad and specific goals); (ii) policy or legal framework4 and



- 44 -                              Annex I
(iii) the proposed implementation plan, detailing all major activities that
will be undertaken under the subcomponent.
SO.   Each subcomponent in the POx is described conceptually according to the
project activities that will be performed by individual teams and costed as
discrete packages. Cross-linkages between activities, timing, landmarks and
paths are presented in descriptive text, through charts (using Gantt Charts,
Pert Charts and Resource Histograms) and in tables. The detailed description
for each major subcomponent of the Project answers all of the following
questiones what, why, how, by who, when and where. The preparation,
management and implementation arrangements of each subcomponent describe the
responsible and implementing organizations (institutions, agencies or
individuals). Names of known institutions and individuals who would be
responsible for organizing and implementing are clearly indicated for each
subcomponent, Where such organizations or individuals have not yet been
identified, the selection process is clearly specified. The POX also
identifies and quantifies the resources that will be used during the remainder
of project preparation, early project start-up and implementation in terms of
investment costs and estimated incremental recurrent expenditure.
51.  Further details are provided in the draft TORs for all major technical
assistance assignments. The draft TORs detail the anticipated objective of
the assignment, scope of work (phases I, 1I, III, etc.), action matrix, timing
and duration of the assignment, deliverables, qualifications and experience of
staff, selection criteria and possible short list of appropriate organizations
or firms. These draft TORs will be reviewed, updated and finalized before
issuing the request for proposals. Finally, criteria for measuring and
evaluating the performance of the proposed intervention based on both meeting
the project development objectives (i.e. improving health indicators) and
success in accomplishing individual tasks (inputs and outputs) have been
provided for each subcomponent (see Section IV of the PIP for a summary of
these performance indicators.
D. Interacency Coordination
52.   The successful implementation of several subcomponents will depend on
close coordination among several different agencies in the public and the
private sector. Primary health care training and activities under the Basic
Health Care Interventions subcomponent will require close cooperation between
the selected management firm, the relevant MOH Gds, and the Central, Regional,
and Provincial Training Teams. A private firm will also be contracted to
assist in the coordinatlon and implementation of the computerLzed management
support systems in the health sector. The Health Services Management TraLLng
subcomponent will require external assistance from a Turkish university
supported by a foreign university. The university will have to work closely
with the appropriate KOH GDs, provincial health directorates and the six pilot
hospitals. The National Burden of Disease (NDB) study will be carried out by
another Turkish university or national institution with support from a foreign
university. since this study will be conducted nationwide, coordination
between all Provincial Health Directorates will be required. A number of UN
specialized agencies may also play a role in the implementation of the



- 45 -                                  Ane_
Project. On-going programs of NOH supported by WHO and UNICEF will be closely
monitored to identify areas where Project activities could benefit from
lessons learned. Experience under the First Health Project has shown that the
PCU can play an effective role in ensuring that there is coordination between
the various agencies. Adequate technical assistance is provided under the
Project to continue to support this critical role for the PCU.
E. Role of the World Bank Durina Supervision
53.   The Project will, on average, require about 25 staff-weeks of Bank
supervision per year during the first two years of implementation to launch
the many of the new project-related activities. These include the training of
primary health care workers, introduction of management reforms and
computerized management support systems in hospitals. Whenever possible, Bank
staff will supervise the First and Second Health Projects in the same
missions, thereby realizing economies of scale in utilization of Bank staff
resources. In addition, the administrative arrangements described in para 53
below cover supervision of much of the day-to-day management support provided
to the PCU, and this results in a reduced need for Bank input, as reflected in
the proposed supervision plan below:

F. Administrative Arranaements
54.   Based on the successful administrative arrangements under the First
Health Project, the MOH has decided to continue obtaining administrative and
logistical support for the procurement of goods, works, training and
consultant's services described under this loan in the form of "Technical
Support to Procurement Activities" and "Management Support Services" through
national and/or international institutions hired in accordance with the World
Bank guidelines and procedures. The practice of using a revolving fund under
these contracts will continue. These organizations will ensure timely
payments for basic services, including the salaries of PCU consultants,
contractors, and goods. The MOH will also assume contractual agreements with
selected universities or national institutions for implementation of the
Health Services Management Training subcomponent and the NBD study.
Consulting firms will be contracted to assist in the coordination and
implementation of the Primary Health Care Training, Basic Health Care
Interventions, Management Support Systems and Preinvestment subcomponents.

59.   Procurement of Goods. Vehicle and equipment contracts which are
estimated to cost more than US$400,000 equivalent per contract will be
procured following international competitive bidding (ICB) procedures in



- 51 -                             Annex s
accordance with the Bank's "Guidelines for Procurement Under IDED Loans and
IDA Credits" (May 1992). Bank standard bidding documents for goods which have
recently been developed for Turkey will be used. Equipment (about fifteen
major packages) will be packaged according to related specialized fields.
Vehicles totalling US$10.4 million equivalent will be procured following ICB
procedures. Equipment and vehicles procured through ICa will account for 75
percent of the total value of equipment requirements. The remaining 25
percent of the equipment value will be in packages suitable for procurement
other than ICB. In the comparison of bids for equipment to be procured
through lCB, domestic manufacturers will be allowed a preferential margin of
15 percent, or the existing customs duty applicable to non-exempt importers,
whichever is lower, over the cost, insurance and freight (CIF) prices of
competing imports, in accordance with Paras. 1 through 4 of Appendix 2 of
"Bank Procurement Guidelines" (May 1992). Based on existing relations between
UNICEF and MOH, there may be direct procurement from UNICEF of midwife kits
and disposable refills for midwives at health posts, health centers, and
maternal and child centers, at an estimated cost of US$6.2 million. This
amount will provide a revolving fund for the duration of the Project. It is
anticipated that each midwife would be provided with a new kit on average once
every 18 months.
60.   The exceptions to ICS procurement will be:
(a)   Local Competitive Biddino  LCD)B. LCD procedures, acceptable to
the Bank, would be used for the procurement of equipment and
furniture packages estimated to cost between US$200,000 and
US$400,000 per contract, not to exceed an aggregate amount of
US$5.0 million. This would consist of approximately 13 packages
of medical furniture and nine packages of office furniture for the
mid-sized hospitals and the urban and rural health centers, urban
in-service health training centers, and public health laboratories
supported under the Project. The employment of a management
consulting firm for Primary Health Care Training and Basic Health
Care support will also be made through LCB. Laws and regulations
regarding local procurement procedures and practices in Turkey are
being reviewed in the Bank in order to reach a judgement as to
whether local procedures are acceptable for Bank-financed
contracts. The findings of that review will be discussed with the
Turkish authorities and agreements will be sought by about June
1995 regarding any changes needed to make the procedures
acceptable to the Bank. However, in the meantime, the procedures
outlined in the Standard LCB documents drafted by the Bank will be
followed.
(b)   International Shonoina (IS).  Procedures for IS wiLl be used for
small amounts of equipment where the cost of 1CB will clearly
outweigh possible price advantages. Contracts for equipment
estimated to cost less than US$200,000 per contract, not to exceed
an aggregate amount of US$1.7 million equivalent, will be awarded
under international shopping procedures, based on comparing price
quotations obtained from at least three suppliers from three
eligible countries in accordance with Bank guidelines.



- 52 -
(c)   Local Shonoina ILSI. Minor sundry items not exceeding US$100,000
per contract, up to an aggregate amount of US$2.8 million
equivalent, could be purchased on the basis of prudent local
shopping by comparing price quotations obtained from at least
three local suppliers, in accordance with procedures acceptable to
the Bank. This procurement method provides a rational set of
procedures acceptable to the Bank to purchase small items which
cannot be suitably packaged for LCB.
(d) Direct Procurement. Goods and technical licenses which the Bank
agreess (i) are of proprietary nature; (ii) the timely supply
thereof is critical for efficient project execution; or (iii) need
to be compatible with other installed equipment, may be procured
through direct negotiations with property and/or copyright owners,
on terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank. Such items
include contracts for intellectual property such as books,
technical journals,training materials, audio-visual materials,
computer applications software, including annual upgrading and
licensing arrangements, copyrights, translation and reprinting
rights for training materials, estimated to cost about US$1.8
million. Direct procurement also includes an additional US$6.2
million for UNICEF (see para. 58).
61.  Civil Works.  For civil works, contracts of US$0.5 million and above
will be awarded through international competitive bidding procedures. The
aggregate for ICB will be US$51.8 million. Smaller contracts of less than
US$0.5 million are not expected to attract foreign bidders and therefore will
be awarded through local competitive bidding procedures for firms which have
been pre-qualified, based on criteria acceptable to the Bank. The aggregate
for LCB will be US$1.6 million. Standard ICB and LCD bidding documents have
recently been prepared by the Bank for use in Turkey, and will be used for
civil works under the Second Health Project. Force account procedures will
only be used; (ii) when no satisfactory bids have been received; or (ii) when
LCB is not likely to succeed, as in the case of small buildings in remote
areas or minor remodelling.
62.  Technical Assistance.  Qualified firms will be invited to submit
proposals for comprehensive packages of consultant services and management of
fellowships and training in accordance with the "Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank as Executing Agency"
(August 1981). To keep the evaluation process manageable, no more than six
(but at least three) proposals will be invited following a short listing
acceptable to the Bank. As needed, the services of individual specialists not
to exceed US$100,000 per contract (mainly to develop computer applications
software) will also be contracted directly. It is estimated that technical
assistance will be procured in 18 packages at a total cost of US$31.8 million.
Two of these packages will include. the selection of Turkish universities or
national institutions, supported by local and foreign universities and
consulting firms to carry out the Health Services Management Training
subcomponent and the National Burden of Disease atudy. The Turkish
universities or national institutions will be selected by MOH using procedures
acceptable to the Bank. All technical assistance services will be procured in
keeping with draft terms of reference described in the PON. Implementing units
will provide adequate technical input to the PCU in the selection process.
Prior review of consultant contracts shall not apply to (a) contracts for



- 53 -                              Annex 1
consultant firms estimated to cost less than US$100,000 equivalent each or
(b) contracts for the employment of individuals estimated to cost less than
US$100,000 equivalent each. However, the TORe of such contracts will be pre-
reviewed.
63.   Pro-investment and Development Studies.  Included in the above technical
assistance aggregate, the Project will provide funds (US$8.2 million) under
the Pre-Investment Studies and Project Management subcomponent for
studies/research/surveys related to a piloting/implementation of the
Government's 1HCR Program. Awards of contracts to institutions/specialists
for these studies will be in keeping with the provisions of the Bank
Guidelines for the Selection of Consultants.
64.   In compliance with Bank procedures, standard model bidding documents and
prior review will be used for all ICS procurement. The Bank will continue to
review master lists of equipment, packaging of bids and updated cost
estimates. According to procedures set forth in paragraphs 2 and 4 of
Appendix 1 of the Guidelines, the Bank will review under each subcomponent the
first two contracts for goods to be procured under LCB and estimated to cost
the equivalent of between US$200,000 and US$400,000. It is estimated that this
prior review will cover about 80 percent of the value of all contracts
combined for equipment and technical assistance. The remaining 20 percent of
contracts will be subject to selective post award reviews by the Bank during
the supervision of the Project. The Bank will review only TORs for consultant
contracts under US$100,000 equivalent; above this threshold standard, World
Bank review procedures will apply. If applicable, civil works contracts of
US$500,000 or more will be subject to prior review and approval by the Bank.
65.   The MOH has also requested retroactive contracting of up to US$10.0
million and financing of up to a maximum of US$2.0 million to cover
expenditures incurred between appraisal and project effectiveness to support
the following activitiess (a) technical assistance to support coordination of
the primary health care training programs and coordination of programs such as
the Safe Motherhood and Midwifery Training Program; (b) technical assistance
from Turkish institution(s) to support implementation of the health services
management training program; (c) technical assistance to support
implementation of the management support systems subcomponent; (d) technical
assistance from Turkish institutions to support implementation of a National
Burden of Disease assessment and cost-utility studies in Turkey; and (e)
equipment for selected health posts, health centers, and maternal and child

D. Financial and Economic Analysis
69.   Shifting emphasis from hospital to ambulatory-baBed primary health care
is expected to reduce future cost-escalating tendencies, while at the same
time orienting the health sector to more affordable and cost-effective
interventions. Cost utility studies, however, would be undertaken subsequent
to the first round of NBD calculations, and updated in the light of findings
of the second round. The main components of the cost utility methodology will
be: (a) the identification of disease priorities (which is an output of the
NBD study); (b) identification of potentially effective interventions against
the most important causes of morbidity and mortality; (c) a determination of
the impact of those interventions; (d) a measurement of the costs of those
interventions; and (e) the combination of impact and cost information to
produce a cost per Disability Adjusted Live Year (DALY) gained for each
intervention. Since the DALY is a rather insensitive measure at the level of



- 57 -                              AnnexJl
specific interventions, more specific outcome measures will also be used to
derive cost-effectiveness measures (see Section IV of this Annex).
70. The cost utility studies would be undertaken under the same broad
institutional auspices as the NBD studies, because of the need for consistency
in the assumptions concerning the baseline epidemiological situation and the
impact of interventions. Therefore, both the Turkish institutions and an
international collaborating institution will need to assemble a capacity
ranging across the disciplines of demography, epidemiology, statistics and
health economics, to conduct these studies.
E. Audit Timetable
71. Proiect Audits. Project Accounts and the Special Account will be
audited in accordance with the Bank's "Guidelines for Financial Reporting and
Auditing of Projects Financed by the World Bank" (March 1982). The Borrower
will provide the Bank (within six months of the end of each fiscal year), an
audit report of such scope and detail as the Bank may reasonably request,
including a separate opinion by an independent auditor acceptable to the Bank,
on disbursements against certified SOEs. The separate opinion should mention
whether the SOEs submitted during the fiscal year, together with the
procedures and internal controls involved in their preparation, can be relied
upon to support the related withdrawal applications. TORs for the scope of
such audits have been developed under the First Health Project.
72. Revortina and Annual Reviews. The PCU will prepare semi-annual
descriptive and financial reports on each project component, objective and
activity, beginning from the date of Loan Effectiveness. The reports will
describes (a) current status; (b) deviations, if any, from the implementation
plan; and (c) the reasons for deviations and corrective actions being taken.
Sample progress-reporting documents are included in the POX. Reporting and
Bank supervision will focus on in-depth reviews of the policy framework for
sectoral improvements, the consistency of activities for each component and
subcomponent with agreements reached at negotiations, performance of the
implementing groups and institutions, assessment of emerging needs for
adjustments to project parameters, and possible follow-up operations which
could be supported by the Bank. Bank supervision will be based on the semi-
annual progress reports and the annual evaluation of project activities by the
NOR. This will be supplemented by a review of project expenditures and
availability of financial resources, in addition to substantive discussions
with technical assistance groups implementing and/or assisting in the
implementation of various components, and discussions with responsible
authorities. The POX will be the basis for supervision and reporting.



- 58 -                              Annex I
F. Main Proiect Risks
73. A major risk faced by the Project is that it may become difflcult to
implement and supervise activities in a number of the project provinces which
currently have accessibility problems. Contingency plans are included to
allow: (a) out-of-province training where necessary; (b) pre-selection in
problematic provinces of only urban facilities for new civil works to ensure
the possibility of an adequate supervision, staffing and supply of equipment;
(c) province-by-province coating to allow flexibility under a phased
implementation plan; and (d) use of a professional engineering firm to oversee
the construction programs. A second risk is the limited institutional
capacity of MOH line departments, especially in the area of training. This
risk would be mitigated by: (a) integration of staff trained through
fellowships financed under the First Health Project; (b) technical assistance,
including management contracts for logistic support in coordinating training
activities; (c) decentralization of training efforts to provincial teamsl
(d) modular distance-based education through TV and local discussion groups;
(e) improved coordination of the different General Directorates in an
integrated approach to training activities; and (f) collaborations with
universities as co-participants in the institutionalization of the training
programs.
G. other Factors Affecting Proiect's Success
74. There are a number of critical factors that could have an adverse affect
on the success of the Project. These include the followings
(a) High turnover rates of health personnel in Eastern Turkey;
(b) inadequate logistical systems to ensure that primary health care
facilities are adequately equipped, and supplied with drugs and
other consumables;
(c) lack of resources to extend basic health care programs to other
parts of the country; and
(d) inadequate budget requirements for investment and recurrent costs.
H. Loan Covenants for Implementation
75. Section V of the Staff Appraisal Report shows a complete list of loan
covenants to be monitored during the Project. The following covenants require
special attention to mitigate the risks indicated in Section 0 aboves

implementation of programs similar to the programs referred to

b.   The Borrower shalls (a) ensure that the Project shall be carried

agreed upon between tl)e Borrower and the Bank, as the same may be

including in the management of the implementation thereof, the
design and construction supervision of the works thereunder and the
procurement of goods, works and services therefor; (c) ensure (i)
the adequate deployment and centinuity of primary health care
personnel in the Project Provinces and (li) the adequate equipping
and supplying with drugs and other consumables of the primary
heal*h care facilities located in the said provinces, all in

Operations Manual; and (d) ensure that the training activities

including time-schedules for the implementation thereof and
criteria for the selection of candidates thereunder, satisfactory
to the Bank; and
a.   throughout the Project cycle, the Borrower will appropriate
sufficient funds to ensure the effective implementation of the
investment program, especially with respect to the annual
construction of approved facilities and incremental recurrent
expenditure for salaries, drugs and needed consumables in the
implementing institutions.
IV. MONITORING AND EVALUATION
A. Actions to Monitor Develooment Obiectives
76. Tracking key monitoring indicators is necessary to measure the impact of
the project inputs on the key development objectives. These indicators have
been prepared and will be monitored throughout the project cycle (see Section
IV below). At the mid-term review, the need to fine-tune or restructure the
Project design will be based on the data received from these indicators.
B. Development Indicators
77. Given the essential role program monitoring and evaluation play in
determining the impact of a given intervention on development objectives,



- 60 -Am=-&
three sets of indicators will be used to monitor and evaluate progress during
implementation of the Second Health Project: input indicators, output
indicators and outcome indicators. In addition, the Project will initiate the
first NBD asseasment in the Region which will provide a comprehensive revlow
of Turkey's main morbidity and mortality patterns (see Annex 4 for details).
78. outcome Indicators will track whether or not the development objectives
of the Project have been met. Two criteria were used to select the basic
outcome indicators for health statues (a) that the indicator is known to be
sensitive to the intervention introduced under the projecti and (b) that data
will be readily available through routine collection on a provincial basis
without special studies or surveys. Zn Turkey, only three health status
indicatora, which can be derived from census data, qualify based on these
criteria: (i) infant mortality rates; (ii) fertility rates, and (iii) life
expectancy.
79. For each of the 23 project provinces, improvements in infant mortality,
decreases in fertility rates and increases in life expectancy relative to a
weighted national average, will be used as the ultimate benchmark of success
of the Second Health Project. In addition, the following six outcome
indicators and six intermediate process indicators will be tracked during the
project through survey sampling techniques:
80. Outcome Indicators
(a)  Infant mortality rates                           deor to below 40%
(b)  Under-5 mortality rates                          decr to below 80%
(c)  Child deaths due to ARD                          decr by 30%
(d)  Maternal mortality and case fatality rates       decr by 30%
(a)  Fertility Rates                                  decr by 30%
(f)  Infections due to Tetanus, Polio and Measles    decr by 95%
Intermediate Process Indicators
(a)  Antenatal and postnatal contact rates            inmr to 3 contacts
(b)  Ambulatory health center staffing rates          incr to 95%
(a)  Availability of antibiotics                       100% on spotcheck
(d)  Home DeliNzry/Hospital Delivery Ratios           decr by 10%
(e)  Contraception Prevalence Rates                    inmr by 30%
(f)  Immunization Rates (Tetanus, Polio, Measles)    inar to 95%
81. Throughout the Project, a Monitoring and Evaluation Committee will be
maintained and given responsibility for: (i) tracking the above indicators;
(ii) tracking inputs and outputs as they relate to the project development
activitioul and (iii) overseeing of the NBD and other pro-investment studies.
This committee will be headed by PCU General Coordinator.
C. Proieot Indicators
82. Input Indicatore will track planned investments in terms of contracting
technical assistance (staff-months), enrollment and time (staff-months) in



-61-                               Annex-1
training program  and procurement of goods and civll works (disbursements).
These indicatore will be represented in standard input tables for each major
subcomponent of the Project.
83. Output Ildicators will track items such ass (a) the number of studies or
reports that have been completed by a given date during the project cycle; (b)
the number training programs held and students trained: (c) the equipment or
vehicles that have been procured; and (d) the number of equipped facilities
that have been constructed or renovated. These indicators will be tracked in
standard Output Tables for each major subcomponent of the Project.
84. Input/Output Tables have been prepared for each subcomponent of the
project (see POX). These tables will be used to monitor the progress in
project implementation.
D. Financial Indicators
85. The POX contains detailed finuncial requirements based on the tLming of
major procurement packages. The P. will be responsible for monitorLng and
revising the Plan to ensure there is sufficient lead time to submit realistic
annual budget requirements to the SPO. The PCU w .1 also monitor the affect
of the Project on incremental and recurrent costs. The introduction of modern
management systems to hospital operations will improve the efficiency and
reduce overall unit cost. The Computerized Management Support System
subcomponent will use financial indicators (fiscal balance in the hospital
sector) as a means to measure the success of introducing efficient and cost
effective services.
