- 94 -                             Annoic 4
REPUBLIC OF TURKEY
SECOND HEALTH PROJECT:
ESSENTIAL HEALTH ERVICES AND MANAGEMENT DEVELOPMENT
IN EASTERN AND SOUTHEASTERN ANATOLIA
I.  Monitorina and Evaluation Overview
1.    Health care projects are notoriously plagued by a la',k of reliable
baseline data as well as weak monitoring and evaluation systems. Bank health
projects, in particular, typically focus on project inputs and accountability
issues, such as whether or not the project complied with commitments
established in the Staff Appraisal Report, rather than on outputs and outcome
indicators in terms of improvements in health status or the performance of
health services (effectiveness, efficiency and quality of care). This makes
it difficult to assess the cost-effectiveness and impact of proposed
interventions.
2.    In practical terms, however, measuring the impact of a given
intervention on health status is difficult. For instance, although tracking
maternal mortality rates would be desirable for measuring the impact of the
Second Health Project on the health status of women, routine data for this
indicator is not readily available in Turkey and the relative infrequency of
maternal deaths in the population as a whole makes survey techniques
unreliable unless performed on a large population group which is costly and
labor intensive. Furthermore, information on maternal mortality is most
useful when it is cause-specific, which is rarely the case in developing
countries. As a result of such problems, although pregnancy in developing
countries carries a risk of death up to 200 times greater than in industrial
countries, many of these deaths go undetected, adding to the general
difficulty in assessing cost-effectiveness. It also makes it difficult to
determine if these programs have an impact on the estimated 7 million or more
newborns who die throughout the world as a result of maternal health problems.
3.    Given the essential role program monitoring and evaluation play in
determining the impact of a given intervention on development objectives,
three sets of indicators will be used to monitor and evaluate progress during
implementation of the Second Health Projects input indicators, output
indicators and outcome indicators.
4.    InDut Indicators will track planned investments in terms of contracting
technical assistance (staff-months), enrollment and time (staff-months) in
training programs and procurement of goods and civil works (disbursements).
These indicators will be represented in standard input tables for each major
subcomponent of the Project (see Project Operations Manual).
5.    Output Indicators will track items such as: (a) the number of studies
or reports that have been completed by a given date during the project cycle;
(b) the number training programs and students that have been trained; (c) the
equipment or vehicles that have been procured; and (d) the number of equipped
facilities that have been constructed or renovated. These indicators will be



- 95 -                              Annex 4
tracked in standard output tables for each major subcomponent of the Project
(see Project Operations Manual).
6.    Gutcome lnicatorg will track whether or not the development objectives
of the Project have been met. Two criteria were used to select the basic
outcome indicators for health statuss (a) first, that the indicator is known
tzo be sensitive to the intervention introduced under the project; and (b)
second, that data would be readily available through routine collection on a
provincial basis without special studies or surveys. In Turkey only three
health status indicators, which can be derived from census data, qualify by
these criterion, while at the same time reflecting the priority health
problems in the project provinces: (a) infant mortality rates; (b) fertility
rates; and (c) life expectancy.
7.    For each of the 23 project provinces, improvements in infant mortality,
decreases in fertility rates and increases in life expectancy relative to a
weighted national average, will be used as the ultimate benchmark of success
of the Second Health Project.
8.    Developing countries and Ministries of Health are often encumbered by
donor agencies, which demand the collection of different types of data. To
avoid this problem, while allowing for a more sophisticated assessment, than
would be possible through the above three basic indicators, the Project would
support a limited number of special studies and surveys to track an expanded
set of indicators. These additional indicators fall into two categories: (a)
true outcome indicators (improvements in health status)l and (b) intermediate
outcome indicators of health service activity (desirable utilization patterns,
efficient operation of health care facilities, adequate supplies, improvements
in modern contraceptive methods and increase in immunization rates). The
following six outcome indicators and aix intermediate process indicators would
be tracked during the project through survey sampling techniquess
Outcome Indicators                                          Tarcets
a.    Infant mortality rates                           de'cr by 40%
b.    Under-5 mortality rates                          decr to 80%
c.    Child deaths due to ARD                          decr by 30%
d.    Maternal mortality and case fatality rates       decr by 30%
e.    Fertility Rates                                  decr by 30%
f.    Infections due to Tetanus, Polio aiJ Measles    decr by 95%
Intermediate Process Indicators
a.    Antenatal and postnatal contact rates             incr to 3 cont
b.   Ambulatory health center staffing rates            incr to 95%
c.    Availability of antibiotics                      100% on spotcheck
d.    Home Delivery/Hospital Delivery Ratios           decr by 10%
e.    Contraception Prevalence Rates                   incr by 30%
f.    Immunization Rates (Tetanus, Polio, Measles)    incr to 95%



- 96 -                              Annex4
9.    Since it is impossible to disaggregate the relative importance of the
impact by improvements in clinical interventions on these indicator. from the
impact by improvements in health care management, the proposed indicators
would be used to assess the impact of the Second Health Project as a whole on
health status of tho pepul.tion in the 23 project provinces rather than the
impact of any specuific subcomponents.
10.   For the purpose of developing surveys to meaaure the expanded
indicatore in the 23 project provinces, it will be necessary to deviate from
the usual breakdown of the country into five geographic or five development
regions. The usual breakdown of the country into five geographical regions -
Eastern, Western, Southern, Central and Northern Anatolia - does not function
well as a basis for classifying the provineqs according to needs by bealth
indicators (see Map 1). The SPO has, therefore, grouped the country's
provinc s into four different regions based on demographic, income,
employment, education, morbidity and mortality indicators (see Map 2): (a)
first order priority provinces (PP1); (b) second order priority provinces
(PP2); (c) provinces with medium levels of developmentt and (d) developed
provinces. The 23 provinces selected for the Second Health Project are
economically the least developed PPl and PP2 provinces not already covered in
the Eastern Anatolian Region during the First Health Project, whose climate
and geographical conditions significantly weaken accessibility to health
services, especially in remote and mountainous areas (see Map 3). The project
provinces cover 16 percent of Turkey's total population (9 million people in
1990) and 29 percent of its surface area.
11.   The following pages provides (a) the 52 variables used by SPO to
classify provinces according to provincial groups by levels of developmentp
(b) provinces grouped by geographical regions used in Hacettepe Population and
Health Surveys; (c) provinces grouped by levels of development; and (d) basic
indicators by province groups. To monitor outcome during project
implementation, a similar set of tables would be developed, comparing the key
indicators in the 23 project provinces with the rest of the country.

II.  National Burden of Disease Methodoloav for Second Health Proiect
12.   As a more comprehensive assessment of health status of the population,
the Second Health Project will conduct a National Burden of Disease (NBD)
asosesment in Turkey and cost-utility studies. This would ensure that future
reforms in the health sector focus adequately on reducing the burden of
illness due to preventable and treatable diseases and disability in Turkey.
The methodology for this measure will be based on the work of Murray and
Lopes, 1990, during preparation of the 1993 World Development Report.  The
methodology combines mortality and morbidity into a single internationally
comparable unit, the Disability Adjusted Life Year (DALY).1/ This would
assist the Ministry of Health in determining current health priorities and,
when combined with in country cost-utility studies, make it possible to
determine affordable cost-effective packages of health interventions that
would address a significant proportion of preventable and treatable disease
throughout the country. The methodology for the proposed study, which is
summarized in Annex B of the 1993 WDR, is provided for reference in this Annex
of the Staff Appraisal Report. Two assessments, at five year intervals would
be carried out iuring the duration of the project.
13.   Under the Second Health Project, the Ministry of Health will seek
assistance from a Turkish institution or consortium of institutions to manage
and implement the NBD study. This Turkish institution will be oupported by an
overseas institution or consortium of institutions with experience in the NBD
methodology. The Turkish institution will hare final responsibility for
project outputs, and for the coordination of both Turkish and international
expertise. The following terts-of-reference (TORs) can be adapted as
appropriate to either the Turkish host institution(s) or the international
supporting institution(s).
Dbiectives
14.   The overall objective of this assignment will be to:
a.     conduct a NBD study in Turkey which, in conjunction with data oa
the efficiency of specific interventions, can provide the basis
for the rational allocation of resotrces in the health sector;
b.     undertake a series of cost-utility studies that will focus an
conditions identified as priorities by the NBD study and
determine the most cost-effective interventions to address these
priorities; and
c.     develop the institutional capacity in Turkey to undertake NED
and cost utility studies.
i/    Tables 8.1 to 8.5 frm Amex B of the 1993 WN are prowided as an attachmit to this Anmx a
exuples of the Burdmn of Disease by age, sex nd mjor diseae category.



- 105 -                            ADnne  4
Broad 8come
15.   The scope of the study will consist of three major sets of activities
which will be conducted in two phases over the seven and a half yearas (a) MID
studies; (b) cost-utility studies; and (c) capacity building.
16.   NE) Study.  The first major set of activities relates to the NBD study
itself. The NBD study requires demographers to determine the existing
population and overall mortality (1993) by age and sex and to model changes
over the coming decades. This data will be combined with cause-specific
mortality and morbidity estimates by incidence and fatality for a similar
range of diseases as laid out in the WDR 1993 (World Bank 1993), in order to
calculate the DALY loss by disease category and population component. Where
data is not available, estimates will be made by in-country disease experts
making clearly explicit assumptions as to how these estimates were derived.
17.   The NBD study will include the following detailed activities:
a.     Organize NMD launch workshop to include a presentation of the
World Development Report.
b.     Organize NBD study design workshop.
c.     Undertake a study of demographic data for the year 1993 by age
and sex, to determine population size and mortality in the
following age bands 0-4 yrs, 5-14 yrs, 15-44 yrs, 45-59 yrs. and
60+ yrs.
d.     Produce estimates for incidence and fatality from dtseases in
three broad groupingss (i) Communicable, maternal & perinatal;
(ii) noncommunicable; and (iii) injuries. Bach grouping will be
broken down into their individual diseases (Iyrox. 60). These
estimates will be sought from a wide variety of existing sources
including, vital registration data, clinic and hospital data,
and special studies.
e.     Combine activities under (c) and (d) to produce a statement of
the NBD, expressed in DALYs.
f.     Review how the NBD findings compare with the MEC estimates for
WDR 1993, and draw out implications for the establishment of
health priorities. Present findings to Ministry of Xealth, and
hold i-ional seminar to promote study impact.
g.     Undertake the design and contracting of four supp'ementary
morbidity and mortality studies during the project period to
fill priority data gaps identified during the first (1993) MND
assessment in order to improve the quality of the second MID
(1998) assessment. TA will be provided as required to this
exercise.
h.     Repeat exercise, centered around the 1998 situation, five years
after original study.



- 106 -                             annex 4
18.   Cost-utilitv 8tudies.  The second major set of activities relates to
the accompanying cost-utility studies. The cost-utility studies would be
undertaken subsequent to the first round of NBD calculations, and updated in
the light of findings of the second round. The coct-utility studies includes
(a) the identification of disease priorities (which is an output of the NMD
study under the first component); (b) the identification of potential
interventions against the selected conditions, and the determination of the
impact of those interventions; (c) the measurement of the costs of those
interventions; and (d) the combination of impact and cost information to
produce the measure cost per DALY gained for each intervention. Since the
DALY is a rather insensitive measure at the level of specific interventions,
more specific outcome measurea will also be used to derive cost-effectiveness
measures.
19.   It is envisaged that the cost utility studies would be undertaken under
the same broad institutional auspices as the NBD studies, because of the need
for consistency in the assumptions concerning the baseline epidemiological
situation and the impact of interventions. Therefore, both the Turkish host
institution and the international collaborating institution will need to
assemble a capacity ranging across the disciplines of demography,
epidemiology, statistics and health economics, if necessary by the formation
of consortia.
20.   The cost-utility studies will include the following de>-Ailed
activities:
a.     On the basis of the first NBD study, identify likely candidates
for intervention programs. These are to be selected on the
basis of both a high loss of DALYs and the judgement that there
currently exist (or will shortly become available) effective low
cost technologies against the selected conditions.
b.     Conduct a launch workshop on cost utility studies to disseminate
knowledge of the methodology, to debate contentious issues of
the methodology to be adopted in the Turkish studies, and to
review the selection of interventions to be studied.
c.     Using tlhe available data and the judgement of the expert peer
review group, construct estimates of the impact of intervention
programs, measured in DALY units and more specific outcomes.
d.     Estimate the cost of the intervention programs.
e.     Combine outputs of r) and s) above to generate estimates of cost
per DALY gained, and estimates of cost effectiveness, and
present results to national health policy workshop.
f.     Repeat the cycle in abbreviated form following second NBD study,
updating previous intervention studies and adding selected new
intervention programs.

21.   Capacity buildino.  The third major set of activities rela.es to the
accompanying cost-utility studies.   This will include: (a) the es;ablishment
of a NBD Steering Committee; (b) national capacity building; and (c) foreign
contractor.
22.   Establlshment of a NBD SteerLng Committee. The Ministry of Health will
create a Committee, whose secretariat will be provided by the Project
Coorlination Unit, to oversee the NBD and cost-utillty studies. This
Committee will be responsible for major decisions concerning the studies,
including: (a) selection of the contractors; (b) approval of the annual
workplan and budget proposed by the project coordinator; (c) selection of
candidates for PhD and MSc fellowships and approval of the terme of their
fellowships; (d) selection of the members of the expert panel; le)
facilitation of access to official statistical sources; and (f) reporting to
the World Bank on the progress of the studies. The Committee will also be
responsible for resolving any disputes arising from the division of
responsibilities between the Tuxkish 'nstitution and the international
collaborating institution. It is anticipated that the NBD Steering Committee
will need to meet frequently during the initial period of establishment of the
Project. After the first year, the frequency of meeting should be specified
as not less than twice annually, and additionally at the request of the
Droject Coordinator.
23.   National Capacity Building.  The Turkish institution or consortium to
which this contract is awarded will be required to provide a comprehnnsive
package of inputs which, in conjunction with She technical expertise to be
provided under a separate contract with an international collaborating
institution, will be sufficient to undertake tha activities and produce the
outputs enumerated below. In particular, the institution will be expected to
provide: (a) a full-time NBD Coordinator who will be responsible for managing
the entire process of conducting the various studies; (b) counterpart
professional staff as needed in the disciplines of epidemiology, demography,
medical statistics and health economics who will work alongside experts from
the international institution; (c) support staff; and (d) accommodation with
computer facilities and international communications for the use of the
various working teams. The NBD Coordinator will be expected to manage the
payment of honoraria to the expert panel, the employment of field survey
assistants, the organization of project workshops and seminars, and the
publication of reports on the project. The NBD Coordinator will also be
expected to put forward nominations for the PhD and Msc fellowships, and to
design and implement the arrangements by which fellows are surpported in their
studies and engaged in the activities of the project.
24. The project coordinator will be expected to prepare for the approval of
the Project Steering Committee an annual budget and workplan, detailing
proposed activities, necessary inputs and expected outputc for the forthcoming
year. On conclusion of the project year, the project coordinator will prepare
a report on performance and budget for the Steering Committee
25.   The relationship between the Turkish and the international institution
is intended to be one of intellectual collaboration. The international
institution will supply inputs of technical assistance according to a schedule
laid down in their contract. The precise timing of those inputs, and the
scope of work for individual visits, will be negotiated between the NBD
Coordinator and the international team leader. In the event of irreconcilable
differences, matters in dispute may be referred to the NBD Steering Committee.

26.   Forelgn Contractor.  The foreign contractor for the NBD and cost-
utility studies has two main tasks. The first is to supply the technical
skills and experience to guarantee that high quality research is performed and
published. The second is to ensure that there is a transfer of skills to the
Turkish counterparts, research assistants and study fellows engaged in the
project, and to a wider audience through workshops and seminars. The
structure of the NBD and cost-utility studies makes the Turkish NBD
Coordinator "de jure" responsible for all project outputs, and for the
coordination of all inputs (including international technical assistance
within the framework of the contract), but "de facto" the contractor's
personnel will have a large indirect supportive role to play in conducting the
studies.
27.   Capacity building will include the following detailed activitie0s
a.     Establish and equip accommodation provided by Turkish
institution and recruit additional staff as necessary.
Designate Turkish counterparts to all international staff.
b.     Recruit one PhD student each in demography, epidemiology/public
health, and health economics, arrange their attachment to the
project, and nrovide PhD scholarships in an appropriate overseas
institution.
C.     Establish expert Peer Review Group for disease estimates.
d.     Develop a detailed plan of local project costs, including
travel, secretarial and office costs and honorariums for disease
experts and additional estimators.
e.     Hold a series of workshops to explain methodologies to Turkish
ac.%demics and policy makers, and to present the results of
studies (these workshops are listed separately under NBD and
cost utility sub-activit.es).
f.     Under the umbrella of the coordinating institution the NID
coordinator would be responsible for overseeing the technical
work in cooperation with the technical review committee and the
participating institution providing technical assistance to the
project.
g.     In particular the NBD Coordinator would be responsible for
coordinating the varied institutional inputs into the project,
liaising closely with the overseas institution providing
technical suppc-.t and be responsible for production of all
reports relating to the project, ensuring publication in both
Turkish and English language versions.

Timina and Duration
29. This is a seven and a half year study commencing in 1995 and finishing
end 2001. It is intended that the contracts will be awarded in 1994, allowing

concerned with the NBD studies, and again in 1999 and 2000. In 1997 and 1998,
lt is anticipated that 4 special studies to strengthen the disease date will
be undertaken. Cost utility studies will be undertaken in 1997 and 1998, and
updated and extended in 2001 in the light of the results of the second NBD

oualifications
30. The institutions bidding for this contract will require expertise in
the areas of epidemiology, demography, medical statistics (analysis of
morbidity and mortality data), health economics, and field research methods
(survey design, execution and data analysis). The NBD Coordinator and
principal investigators should have sound academic training and a record of
published research in the relevant fields. The foreign institution should
have a demonstrated previous involvement in the NBD effort and previous record
of performance in commissioned resea_rch and international collaboration.
Seloction Criteria
31.   Proposals made by bidding institutions will be judged on the revealed
capacity to coordinate and execute the project, to supply the required
personnel and guarantee their availability for appropriate durations, to
supply the support staff, and to have available (or to indicate the source of)
the accommodation and services required by the project team.
32.   It is unlikely that a single institution will have sufficient capacity
in all these areas of expertise, and it is therefore expected that bidders
will form consortia.   In this event, the bids will be judged additionally on
the arrangements made between institutions to guarantee coordination} bidders
will need to supply copies of the agreements made between the memoers of the
consortium, and these must clearly show commitment to the designated NOD
Coordinator and a readiness to accept his coordinating role. The bid must
clearly demonstrate the commitment of the institution and the individual to
engage the NBD Coordinator on a full time basis.

Attachment Ouoted from Annex B
World Development Report WDER) 1993lnvesting in Health
11.   "The World Bank and the World Health Organization have undertaken a
joint exercise for this Report that quantifies the impact in loss of healthy
life from about 100 diseases and injuries in 1990 (Murray and Lopez,
background paper). The global burden of disease (GBD) combines the loss of
life from premature death in 1990 with the loss of healthy life from
disability. The OBD is measured in units of disability-adjusted life years
(DALYs) except in Table B.8, which presents only deaths. Disease and injury
categories are based on the Internatlonal Classification of Diseases, Ninth
Revlslon (1977). The criterion for selecting the diseases and injuries
studied was the expected magnitude of the burden within a specific age group.
The selected diseases and injuries account for more than 90 percent of
premature deaths and probably for a similar proportion of the burden
attributable to disability. The tables presented in this appendix include
subticals for different disease groups; the numbers presented in the subtotals
include DALYs lost as a result of the specified diseases and, in some cases,
several residual conditions."
12.   "Calculation of the disease burden is based on several assumptions,
some of which involve decisions about ethical values or social preferences.
The key choices are the potential years of life lost as a result of a death at
a given age; the relative value of a year of healthy life lived at different
ages; the discount rate, or extent of time preference for human life and
health; and the disability weights used to convert life lived with a
disaLility to a common measure with premature death. The choices are
described below. A full presentation of the methodology will appear in Murray
and Lopez (forthcoming)."
13.   "Duration of life lost due to a death at each aae.  The number of years
of life lost as a result of a death at each age is obtained from a standard
schedule of expectations of life at that age. A Coale and Demeny (1983)
"West" family model life table (level 26 with an excectation of life of 82.5
years) has been used as the standard for females. A comparable model with an
expectation of life of 80 years has been used as the standard for males. For
example, a female death at age 40 is assumed to represent a stream of lost
life that is equal to the female expectation of life at age 40, or 43 years.
Longer streams of life lost as a result of a female death as compared with a
male death at any given age seem to be justified by data for high-income
groups in low-mortality populations. These data show that women's
expectations of life at birth are still two to three years higher than
males'."
14.   "Value of a health year of life lived at each aae.  Most societies
attach more importance to a year of life lived by a young or middle-aged adult
than to a year lived by a child or an elderly person. The relative value of a
year of life at each age has been modeled for this exercise as an exponential
function of the form ka exp(-Ba), where a is age and B is equal to 0.04. This
function rises quickly from zero at birth to a peak at age 25 and then
declines asymptotically toward zero. The constant k is chosen so that the
total number of DALYs is the same as though uniform age weights had been used
(see Box figure 1.3).  It is important to note that while the first year of
life receives a very low weight, the life a newborn is valued accordlng to the
weights of all the years he or she is expected to live, that is, according to
the sum of the function over future years. In the absence of discounting,



- 112 -                             AneL 4A
therefore, the greatest lose of DALYs from premature death occurs from infant
deaths. The introduction of discounting means that the greatest loss from
premature death occurs in early adulthood, but that loss is only slightly
greater than the loss from an infant death. Largely because loss of life is
valued according to the future stream of age-specific age weights and not just
the weight for one year, the results of the analysis are not very sensitive to
the introduction of nonuniform age weights."
15.   "Time preference.  Since the stream of damage to health from current
illness and injury can extend years or even decades into the future, it must
be decided how to value the future relative to the present. This is a
controversial and unresolved issue becsuse there are two different arguments
concerning how to regard the future. first, societies typically prefer to
have a given amount of consumption today rather than tomorrow. This "pure
social rate of time preference" is usually assumed to be quite low, of the
order of 0-3 percent per year, meaning that the future is valued exactly or
almost as much as the present. Second, there is a reason for discounting the
future much more, if resources are not consumed today but are invested so as
to generate higher consumption tomorrow. The expected rate of growth of
consumption times the elasticity of utility with respect to consumption yields
a term typically assumed to be about 8-10 percent, comparable to the rate of
return on investments involving risk and taxation. In evaluating the global
burden of disease, it is assumed that this argument does not apply to human
lives, which are incommensurable with consumption; adding to healthy years
does not necessarJ.ly raise consumption per person.  This Report therefore uses
a discount rate of 3 percent per year, which could be entirely attributed to
pure time preference."
16.   "Higher discount rates would reduce the total burden of diaease because
future health damage from health losses in 1990 would account for less. More
important, higher discount rates would also alter the relative importance of
different diseases. Because the stream of life lost as a result of mortality
is, on average, longer than that caused by disability, higher discount rates
raise the importance of disability compared with that of premature mortality.
For the same reason, higher discount rates reduce the importance of premature
deaths at young ages in relation to those at older ages."
17.   "Disability weiohts.  Disabilities were assigned severity weights
ranging from zero, representing perfect health, to one, representing death.
These disability weights were determined at a meeting of experts in
international health who had not participated in the study. In order to
reduce the number of weights to be assigned and to emphasize large differences
in the severity of disability, each disability condition was assigned to one
of six classes of severity. The disabilities in a particular class differ in
kind (for example, blindness versus paralysis) but were considered to be of
equal severity. Each participant then voted on the weight to be assigned to
the entire class, not to individual disabilities, and the class was weighted
according to the average vote. It is important to note that many disabling
conditions lead to two or more distinct disabilities, which may be classified
in more than one class of severity."
18.   "The burden of disease could be computed using a prevalence perspective
(the extent of burden during a given year, no matter when a disease condition
began) or an incidence perspective (the future burden of that year's new cases
or incident diseases). An incidence perspective was chosen: the burden of
disease is the future stream of disability caused by incident cases in 1990.
This is the more logical way of dealing with mortality, and it is easy to



- 113 -                            Annex 4
apply to nonfatal disabilities. Disability-adjusted life years attributable
to premature mortality are calculated on the basis of 1990 deaths by cause, as
presented in Table B.8."
19. "Estimates of mortality by cause were based on three types of sources
vital registration data, model-based estimates, and epidemiological estimates
for particular diseases. Vital registration data for all countries with good
registration systems that attribute a cause of death were used with only minor
modificationsl such countries include most of the established market
economies, the formerly socialist economies of Europe, and a large proportion
of the countries of Latin America and the Caribbean. For China high-quality
sample registration data from the Disease Surveillance Points System were used
with some modifications. Vital registration data were also used for some
countries in the Middle Zastern crescent and Other Asia and islands regions."
20.   "A variety of models relating cause-specific mortality to total
mortality by age have been developed on the basis of the patterns of causes of
death recorded in nations with good registration systems. For the large
groups of causes-communicable plus maternal and perinatal, noncommunicable,
and injuries-these models have been used to allocate deaths to cause groups.
Whenever possible, these estimates have been validated by examining the
results of small-scale longitudinal population ssrveillance systems.
Estimates for mo-e detailed causes were built ul !rom assessments by disease
experts of incidence, remission, and case-fatality rates. These
epidemiological estimates by cause have been constructed so as to add up to
total mortality."
21.   *A different approach was used to estimate the DALYs lost through life
lived with a disability. A group of experts estimated the incidence, age
onset, and duration of disability for each specific disease on the basis of
community-based epidemiological data, routine health facilities information,
and, where necessary, expert judgement. Separate estimates were made for the
five age groups, two sexes, and eight regions in the study. When prevalence
was used to estimate incidence, the GBD incidence prevalence model was used to
check for consistency between the estimated incidence, remission rate, case-
fatality rate, and general background mortality rate. The same model was also
used to check estimated duration for reach disability. A completed first-
round set of estimates was reviewed at a conference hosted by the World Health
organization, and comments were taken into account. A second round of
estimates was undertaken and was subjected to the same review. The third
round of estimates is presented here. A fourth and final round of revisions
will appear in Murray and Lopez (forthcoming)."

REPUBLIC °F TURKEY
SECOND HEALTH PROJECTs
ESSENTIAL HEALTH SERVICES AND MANAGEMENT DEVELOPMENT
IN EASTERN AND SQUTH EASTERN ANATOLIA
Status Report on The First Health Proiect
A. Backaround/Issues
1.   The Basic Health Care Law of 1961 created a national health service
which was designed to expand health coverage in rural areas. Although the
service was intended to cover the whole country within fifteen years, this
ambitious objective was never achieved. Until the mid-1980s, public health
policy consisted mainly of building hospitals. This left inadequate funds for
equipment, vehicles or major repairs, and only marginal resources for outreach
services. Salaries absorbed most of the operating budgets, leading to
critical shortages of drugs, supplies and fuel, and a general decay of public
health services.   By the 1980s, less than 30 percent of Turkish children were
fully immuni7ed. In 1987, a second Basic Health Care Law was passed which set
the foundation of a general health insurance scheme, and extended the
regulatory functions of the Ministry. Yet, the health sector still lacked the
institutional and financial prerequisites to implement this law in an
equitable manner across the country.
B. The Proiect
2.   t.bjectives.  It was against this background that the Bank first became
involved in supporting the Government's health sector strategy during the mid-
1980s. This led to the preparation of the First Health Project (Loan 3057 TU
f-r USS 75 million) which became effective in October 1990.1/ The objectives
of the First Health Project, which is still under implementation were to: (a)
improve the health status through access for underprivileged populations to
basic health services in eight of the country's provinces (10 percent of the
Population) ;/ (b) enhance efficiency in service delivery and improve health
care financing; and (c) strengthen the management capacity of the Ministry of
Health (MOB).
3.   Detailed Description.  Specifically, the First Health Project financess
(a) construction or expansion of provincial health facilities and training
centerst (b) furniture, equipment, and vehicles for the above facilities; (c)
technical assistance, overseas and local training, and applied research; and
(iv) project monitoring and evaluation. It consists of two major components:
1/   Se Staff Appraisal Report 7433-TU, Jurw 29, 1990 and Sem;-AnuaL Progress Reports through Jure 30,
1993.
if   The eight provinces are Ankara, Diyarbakir, Istanbul, Nardin, Mugla, Sivas, Tokat, and Yozgat.



- 123 -                            Annex fi
a.    Reorganization and Strengthening of Provincial Health Services in
order tos
i.   extend coverage and improve services in eight under-served
provinces with a total population of nine million,3/
through the implementation of health programs, the
strengthening of management, the reorganization of referral
procedures, the upgrading or construction of health
facilities, and the provision of equipment and vehicles;
ii.   achieve a better distribution of health personnel, provide
in-service and induction training for medical and
paramedical staff in the ten provinces, and health education
training for primary school teachers;
b.    Institutional Development through three sub-componentes
i.    health manpower development, focussed on teacher training,
technical assistance for curriculum revision, local and
overseas training, study tours and attendance at
international meetings;
ii. development of a Management Information System, to be first
implemented in the ten project provinces; and
iii. support to the central level, through the strengthening of
HOH's management, planning, monitoring and supervision
capability, applied studies and research, and the
strengthening of the Project Coordination Unit.
4.    The First Health Project aims to reduce the most striking inequalities
in health status in eight provinces and, recognizing the complementary nature
of the MON and the other health care providers, it focusses on the population
not served by social security institutions. These institutions do not provide
coverage for about 25 to 30 million persons who depend almost entirely on the
MOB for their basic health needs. These are not only the rural population.
The figure also includes the urban unemployed as well as the workers of the
informal sector, and their dependents. Therefore, despite the expansion of
private medical facilities, and the projected increase in the number of social
security beneficiaries, the MOB must continue to play a major role, not only
in the provision of preventive services, but also in the coverage of curative
needs of a substantial proportion of the population.
S.   For these reasons, the First Health Project supports the expansion of
maternial and child health, and family planning services, to achieve a greater
impact in the reduction of maternal and infant mortality, as well as
fertility. Lack of access to basic health services and modern contraceptive
V/   Four criteria were used for their selection: (a) basic health indicators about or belou national
average; (b) presence of a strong and motivated technical team; (c) year-rourd accessibility; and
(d) canmfty end political support.



- 124 -                             Annex S
fertility. Lack of access to basic health services and modern contraceptive
methods are major causes of persisting high levels of fertility, short
intervals between births and high infant and maternal mortality rates among
under-privileged groups.
6.    The Project also opens the way for an increase in the revenues derived
from user charges, in order to help sustain the public health system. By
improving the effectiveness of the system, the Project will increase the
number of patients who are willing to pay for better quality services in
public health facilities. 'his will insure a regular flow of resources which
will complement budget allocations and thereby make the system more self-
sufficient.
7.    Finally, the First Health Project paves the way for major institutional
and structural reforms in the health sector. At present, the MOH lacks the
managerial capability to deal effectively with all its sectoral
responsibilities. The institutional development component of the Project is
especially designed to deal with this issue. The First Health Project must,
therefore, be seen as the first step of an important program, very much as a
large-scale pilot operation, designed to support Government's thrust in the
social sectors. It forms the basis for many of the interventions that will be
undertaken in the Second Health Project. Together the first two health
projects would lay the foundations for a more systemic reform of health care
financing which could be supported through an eventual third project in
parallel to or incorporating ongoing reforms in the social security system.
C. Achievements. Remainina Challences. and Lessons Learned
8.    Achievements.  So far, the First Health Project has been successful in a
number of areas. The Project Coordination Unit (PCU) is now fully operational
and all subcomponents of the Project are being implemented. As of July 31,
1994, about US$16.14 million of the Bank loan (21.5 percent) has been
disbursed and another US$13.3 million has been committed. By the end of CY94
about 40 percent of the loan will have been disbursed. Technical assistance
has been instrumental in achieving the objectives of the Project as well as in
preparing for the second and third follow-up projects. Many of the planned
studies have been completed and the remaining are well under way. For
example, the Health Services Utilization Survey, Green Card Survey, HIS
Planning Study, National Development Indices Survey and the Financial Flow
Analysis have been completed. The data from these studies have been
incorporated into the design of the Second Health Project. Similarly,
construction of 98 health posts and S rural health centers has been completed.
Construction of 3 hospitals, 14 urban and 10 rural health centers and 6
training health centers has been started in March 1994. With the completion
of these units by the end of 1994, all of the PHC constructions will have been
completed. Tenders for the remaining 2 hospitals will be put out in November
1994, with a view to complete this work by the end of 1996. Finally,
procurement of medical equipment, furniture and computers is well under way.
Procurement of equipment for PHC facilities has been completed. Tendering
procedures for 246 vehicles out of total 329 have been completed.

10.   Remainino Challenaes.  The Firet Health Project had a slow start-up
period. This was primarily due to four factores (a) unfamiliarity by the
NOR with Bank proceduresl (b) frequent changes in senior management of the
MORH (c) failure to clearly define the role of the PCU in terms of its
relationship to MOR; and (d) weak institutional capacity of the MOR to develop
and implement large in-service training programs. Within the past 12 months
considerable progress has been made in addressing many of these problems.
Today, only one serious outstanding issue remains to be resolved. Although
the PCV has performed well in policy design and procurement, it has largely
failed as an implementing agency for many of the planned training-related
activities and community action programs. As a result, many of these
activities have been transferred back to the MOH as the responsible
implementing agency since it already has an institutional capacity to perform
these activities.
11.   Relevant Lessons Learned.  Several major lessons have been learned
during implementation of the First Health Project which are relevant to and
which are being applied during the design and eventual implementation of the
Second Health Project. First, using the PCU as an implementing agency rather
than as a coordinating unit was initially critical to the success of project
start-up due to the weak institutional capacity of the NOH. In the medium-
term, however, this strategy would prevent the NOH from developing an
institutional capacity to perform these activities itself. During the
implementation of the Second Health Project, an effort would, therefore, be
made to have the PCU reassume the function of a coordinating rather than
implementing unit. Second, successful implementation of the training-related
activities and community action programs would depend on mobilizing the
resources already present in the NOR and in the provinces rather than
duplicating this capacity within the PCU. It also would depend on a smooth
flow of funds and a strong capacity to provide the management and logistical
support needed to implement large scale training activities. Under the Second
Health Project, the Directorates of the NOH would become responsible for
implementing these activities but with the support of a private management
firm to be contracted using Bank procedures. OH and the PCU will continue to
receive support from agencies such as UNDP/OPS, WHO and UNICEF. Third, since
procurement-related activities and civil works are easier to implement than
training-related activities, more effort would be made to ensure that training
would be given equal attention by the PCU. Finally, activities that were well
designed in advance during preparation of the First Health Project have been
implemented much more successfully than those left open-ended. only well
defined activities have been included under the Second Health Project. The
KOH and PCU have prepared a Project Implementation Plan describing all
proposed project activities In detail.
12.   The broad implementation strategy for the remaining disbursements is as
followss (a) medical equipment, constituting 32 percent of the loan, will be
procured after the completion of constructions of the remaining hospital in
1996; (b) 12 percent of loan committed for civil works, which the Government
has decided not to use for this purpose, will be reallocated for technical
assistance and training; and (c) the remaining 16 percent will be disbursed
mainly for vehicles, technical assistance and training during the last two
years of the Project.

REPUBLIC OF TURKRY
SECOND HEALTH PROE:
:4ENTXUA MEALMH SERVICES AND MANAGEMENT DEVELOPMENT
IN EASTERN AND SOUTH EASTERN ANATOLIA
GLOSSARY OF TERMS
Allocative efficiency      - The extent of optimality in distribution of
resources among a number of competing uses.
Average Occupancy Rate     - Number of hospital days/(number of beds x 365)
average Length of Stay     - Number of hospital days/ number of admissions
Child mortality            -  The probability of dying between birth and age
5, expressed per 1,000 live births. The term
"under five mortality" is also used.
Cost-effectiveness
(in health care)           - The not gain in health or reduction in disease
burden from a health intervention ln relation to
the cost. Measured in dollars per disabllity-
adjusted life year.
Crude Birth Rate           - Number of live blrths per year per 1,000 people.
Crude Death Rate           - number of deaths per year per 1,000 people.
Disability-adjusted
life year (DALY)           - A unit used for measuring both the global burden
of disease and the effectiveness of health
interventions, as indicated by reductins in the
disease burden. It is calculated as the present
value of the future years of disability-free
life that are lost as the result of the
premature deaths or cases of disability
occurring in a partlcular year.
Bxternality                - A spillover of benefits or losses from one
individual to another.
Global burden of
disease (GBD)              - An indicator developed for the 1993 World
Development Report, Investlna in Health, in
collaboration wlth the World-Health OrganizatLon
that quantifles the loss of healthy life from
dlsaseg measured ln dlsability-adjusted llfe
years.



-150 -                             G1i  aara
Infant Mortality Rate       -  Annual deaths of infants younger than 1 year old
per 1,000 live births during the same year.
Intervention
(in health care)           -  A specific activity meant to reduce disease
risks, treat illness, or palliate the
consequences of disease and disability.
Life Zxpectancy at Birth   -  The number of yeara a newborn child would live
if subject to the age-specific mortality rates
prevailing at time of birth.
Low Birth Weight (LBW)      -  Infants whose weight at birth is less than 2,500
grams.
Maternal Mortality Rate    -  Number of maternal deaths per 100,000 live
births in a given year attributable to
pregnancy, childbirth, or post-partum.
Median age at death         -  The age below which half of all deaths in a year
occur. This measure is determined both by the
age distribution of the population and by the
age pattern of mortality risks. It does not
represent the average age at which any group of
individual will die, and it is not directly
related to life expectancy.
Population-based
health sorvices             -  Services such as immunization, that are directed
toward all members of a specific population
subgroup.
Rate of Natural Increase   -  The rate at which a population is increasing (or
decreasing) in a given year due to surplus (or
deficit) of births over deaths expressed as a
percentage of the base population.
Rate of Population Growth  -  The rate at which a population is increasing (or
decreasing) in a given year due to natural
increase and net migration, expressed as a
percentage of the base population.
Technical efficiency        -  The extent to which choice and utilization of
input resources produce a specific health
output, intervention,, or service at lowest
cost.



-151 -                             G1osaarx
Tertiary care facility      - A hospital or other facility that offers a
specialized, highly technical level of health
care for the population of a large region.
Characteristics include specialized intensive
care units, advanced diagnostic support
services, and highly specialized personnel.
Total fertility rate        -  The number of children that would be born to a
woman if she were to live to the end of her
childbearing years and bear children at each age
in accordance with prevailing age-specific
fertility rates.
Under Five Mortality (U5M) -  Number of children that die between the ages 1
and 5 per 1,000 live births.
